And two on each knee and two on the ankles, and two on the elbows and two on the clavicles, and cauterize on each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet, and on each heel of his legs, and on each bend of his arms.
And beware of the nerve behind the heels so that you do not burn them, and it may be cauterized with one cautery on the pubic bone and another on the mouth of the stomach and another on the liver, and know that the more you increase the cauteries, the more beneficial and effective they are.
And know that the patient does not feel pain from the cautery as the healthy person does because his body has become numb, and it is necessary to make the cauteries large or small according to the organs and joints as mentioned before in the characteristics of the instruments.
Then he treated the wound with fine honey and other treatment until it healed, God willing.
The Forty-Eighth Chapter.
In a wound of numbness when a member is numbed and treated with medicines, ointments and bandages but does not heal, the affected member should be cauterized according to its size, whether large or small. And let the cautery be thick slightly in the thickness of the skin.
Then he treats it with ointment until it heals, and it may be cauterized for some numbness that occurs in the hand or foot in the vertebrae of the back at the exit of the nerve that moves that member, and the numbness disappears.
And he should not do this except one who is knowledgeable in anatomy of the organs and exits of the nerves that move the body.
The Forty-Ninth Chapter.
In a wound of leprosy.
If leprosy becomes old and no remedy from the art of medicine is effective for it, then cauterize on it deeply a little according to the thickness of the skin until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with fine lentils mixed with rose oil,  camel's tongue fat and pigeon blood or dove blood from each one a part, and they are all mixed together and applied on a cloth and bound to the place until it heals.
The Fiftieth Chapter.
In a wound of cancer.
If the cancer is beginning and you want to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron around it as it revolves. And some sages have mentioned that it should be cauterized with a blunt cautery in its center, and I do not see this because I expect it to scar, and I have seen it several times.
So the right thing is to cauterize around it with a circle as we said or with many cauteries.
Chapter Fifty-One.
Treating the Bite of a Wolf.
If a wolf bite has slowed in healing, whether because of the wound itself, or because of weakness, old age, and little blood, or because of time, and you want to hasten the healing, place small pieces of leeches around it, dripping them frequently.
Then leave them, for they hasten the healing.  If you wish to suck it out with leeches, use the instrument whose picture is shown here.
And place it in the middle of the wound until it pierces the skin, and let the leech be from below so that the pus may flow easily, then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
Treating a Bite of an Animal with Poisonous Saliva.
The 'eater' is a rot that progresses in the limb, eating it like fire consumes dry wood. If you see the 'eater' in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, take many small and large surgical probes and hold them on to the place of the 'eater.'
Then cauterize it from every side until all the rot is eradicated, leaving not even a trace of it. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs and all the rot are removed.
Then treat it with healing ointments for the flesh. If you see after three days that the flesh is growing healthily, without rot, otherwise, repeat the cauterization on what remains of the rotten areas. The 'eater' can also be treated with a strong medication.
For it is faster and more effective than fire. I have mentioned treating it with strong medicine in the previous section; refer to it when you need it.
Chapter Thirty-Five
On the setting of nails [both] reversed and not reversed.
This ailment frequently occurs in the soles of the feet, a harsh thing that afflicts the man, and its treatment is on two faces: either cauterization with fire or cauterization with sharp water.
As for cauterization with fire, it is that you heat a hollow iron instrument resembling the feather of an eagle, made from iron to encompass the nail on all sides and have a thin edge, then lower it over the nail and turn your hand around the nail with the instrument until the instrument reaches the depth of the nail.
And you leave it for three days, [the area] being concerned with pus, then bandage it with a bread poultice pounded with salt and leave the bandage on it overnight; indeed, it will separate from its roots, and then treat the wound site with ointment that is beneficial to flesh until it heals. If the nails are not reversed.
And it frequently occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet; therefore, take a tube from copper or iron or a feather of an eagle, and lower it onto the nail or the spur, then pour into the tube a little bit of sharp water.
And grasp your hand and as you turn it, give a slight twitch to your hand so that the edge of the tube affects the root of the nail, and the sharp water finds its way to dive down to the root of the nail, and let the patient endure the prickling of the sharp water for a while.
Then leave him, and the nail will separate with its roots, so you do this one by one until you come to all of them in the body, then treat their sites after they have separated with ointment that is beneficial to flesh.
Chapter Forty-Four
On the management of fistulas.
If a fistula occurs in one of the nerves due to cold or quarter fever, or otherwise, then it is necessary to make four or five ligatures on the back of the spine between each ligature and in its center. And on its abdomen a ligature with the olive iron, for then the fistula settles and quickly promotes the ripening of the cold disease.
The Fifty-Fifth Chapter.
On ligatures for abscesses that occur in the body.
A foul abscess may appear in the body due to large, cold, putrid materials; when it first appears, it is necessary to apply a gentle ligature on the head of each abscess with a stick that has been ignited at its end with fire or with the root of long saffron or with an optical scalpel.
And the ligatures may be applied on these abscesses in their initial stage; and they must not increase, and then it will subside and the effective cause of it will disappear and the patient will recover from it except that this should be done after bleeding has been performed for the patient.
The Sixty-Sixth Chapter.
On ligatures for bleeding that occurs when an artery is severed.
Often, bleeding from an artery that has been severed during a wound that is exposed from outside or when cutting a tumor or ligating an organ and the like becomes difficult to cut off.
If any of this happens, then quickly put your finger on the mouth of the artery and place your index finger upon it and pull it firmly until it stops the blood from flowing under your finger so that nothing comes out of it; then place several small and large olive irons in the fire until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large, according to the wound and the location where the artery opened, and lower the iron onto the same vein after you quickly remove your finger and hold the iron until the bleeding stops.
If the blood spurts from the artery when you raise your finger and the cauterizing iron goes out, take another cautery quickly from the irons that are in the prepared fire, and keep doing so with one after another until the bleeding stops.
And beware, do not burn a nerve which may be there, for this will cause another affliction on the patient. Know that if an artery bleeds from it, it cannot be cut, especially if the artery is large, except in one of four ways: either by cauterizing as we have said, or by severing it if it has not already been severed. For if it is severed, its two ends will contract and the bleeding will stop. Or, it may be tied with strong thread, or medicinal substances applied to it that are designed to stop blood flow and pulled tightly with forceps.
As for those who attempt to cut it with a ligature or by tightening with a rope, or applying burning substances and the like, it will not stop completely except rarely. If someone finds themselves in this situation and there is no physician or medicine available, they should immediately place their index finger on the mouth of the wound itself as we have described.
And pull it tightly until the blood is constricted and stops flowing from above the wound, and the artery and finger should not be removed from it by cold water continuously until the blood solidifies and stops. During this time, look at what is needed from cauterization or medicine.
Chapter Two.
On incisions, punctures, and surgery of this nature.
Al-Khaliq said: We mentioned in the previous chapter all illnesses that can be treated with cauterization by fire and burning medicine, their causes and reasons, instruments, and images of the cautery irons, and we made those sections from top to bottom.
And I follow in this chapter the same path so that it will be easier for the student to find what they need...
Before I begin this, it is necessary for you to know, O sons of men, that this chapter contains more precious things than what is in the preceding chapter.
Therefore, it is necessary that the warning in this chapter be more severe because the work in this chapter often involves the spilling of blood which is the basis of life when a vein is opened or a tumor is incised, or a fistula is made or a surgical operation is performed or a dart is extracted, or an incision is made on a stone and similar things, all of which are accompanied by danger and fear, and in most of them death occurs.
And I advise you to avoid falling into what is doubtful for you, because there may come to you in this craft kinds of people with various diseases, some of whom have become accustomed to their illness and are close to death.
Because of the severity of what he experiences from his ailment and the length of his suffering, and because of the disease that indicates death, and some of them offer you their wealth and satisfy you with it hoping for health, while his illness is fighting him, so it is not fitting for you to be kind to any of those who possess this characteristic at all.
Let your caution be stronger than your desire and eagerness, and do not proceed with anything of that except after certain knowledge which is established with you as to what will happen to the blessed outcome, and apply in all the treatment of your illnesses  the advance of knowledge and warning as to what will lead to safety.
For in that there is for you aid on the acquisition of praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God inspire you, O sons, with guidance, and not have forbidden you soundness and success, indeed that is by His Hand, there is no deity except He.
And I have arranged this chapter into sections according to what preceded in the chapter of  the body from the head to the foot so that it may be easier for you to obtain what you want from it, God willing.
The First Chapter.
In treating water that collects in the heads of boys.
This ailment is often found in boys at birth if the midwife presses the baby's head roughly, and it can also occur from a hidden cause that is not known.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than boys, and all that I saw from them died quickly, so I saw it as best to leave treatment for it. I did see one boy whose head was filled with water and the head grew larger every day. Until the boy could not sit on himself because of the size of his head and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture is either gathered between the skin and bone or gathered under the bone on the peritoneum, and the treatment for it if the moisture is between the skin and bone and the tumor is small, is to make a single incision lengthwise in the middle of the head, about two finger-widths long, until the moisture drains. And this is the shape of the scalpel.
And if the moisture is more and the tumor is larger, make it two intersecting incisions in this shape:
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from all sides and the water goes down when you press it with your hand inward, and this is not hidden from you, then it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head in this shape.
And after the incision, all the moisture comes out, then you sew the incisions with thread and wool, then apply a poultice of wine and oil to it for five days, then the bandage is removed, and the wound is treated with ointment and dressings.  Do not leave the head tightened excessively, and feed the patient with every dry food that is low in moisture.
Until the member strengthens and heals, another method of incision is to look where the tumor bone appears and the gathering of water because it may be in the back of the head more or at its front or on the right or left.
When you see the swelling and fullness of water, you should make an incision where it appears, as much as you can, taking care not to cut a vein so that bleeding does not occur which could cause death for the patient from the blood loss along with the discharge of moisture.
The Second Chapter.
Cutting the two arteries behind the ears, known as the jugulars.
When a patient experiences severe sudden attacks to the eyes or chest, and this persists despite medication treatment, then cutting these two arteries is the appropriate remedy.
If you wish to cut them, first shave the patient's head with a razor and rub the area with a rough cloth so that the artery becomes visible. Then tie the patient's neck with his garment and look where the sweat pulses and its location, which are the two lower spots behind the ears, which are rarely hidden except in some people.
Then mark them with a stylus and cut them with the scalpel, cutting all the way to the bone. This should be done across the head. If you wish, you can insert the scalpel under the artery and dissect it upward until the cut reaches about two fingers wide when clenched together.
If the sweat is severed, blood will flow out in a pulsating manner, rising forward and then flowing backwards continuously. If the artery is not visible to touch, it is necessary to measure three fingers from the ear and mark it with a stylus, then cut to the bone.
You will send six ounces of blood through the vessel and perhaps more or less, depending on what you see from the patient's strength and fullness of his arteries. Then look at the wound, if some of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it.
So that a hot tumor does not appear, and then tie the wound with a thread of linen cloth and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
The division of the arteries in the ears.
If a person has chronic neuralgia or acute attacks due to harmful moisture and heat in the muscles of the ears, or chronic severe headaches, and various medical treatments have not helped, we have tried in these diseases cutting or cauterizing the arteries in the ears as we described.
And the way to work in cutting them is to shave the hair in the ears, and we mean the artery that appears on the temple, for it becomes clear to you from its pulse, and rarely is it hidden except in individuals.
Or if it is hidden from you due to the intensity of the cold, let the patient tighten his neck with the help of his garment, and then you rub the place with a cloth or cover the place with hot water until the artery appears clearly visible. Then take the scalpel whose image is this. Then you carefully cut the skin with it until you reach the artery, then you place a ligature in it and pull it upwards until you bring it out of the skin and free it from the tissues beneath it on all sides. If the artery is thin, you bend it with the tip of the ligature. Then you cut a portion from it as much as the distance between its ends contracts and does not bleed. If it is not severed, then the blood will not flow at all. Then you drain the blood from six hours to three.
If the artery is large, it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong double thread, let it be either from silk or from bowstrings so that it does not spoil before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then you cut the excess between the ligatures, do this immediately or after some time. And if you wish to cauterize it with a metallic cautery until its ends are burnt and it performs the same function as this operation or better as we have said.
But if the patient has a fever or an imbalance in his temperament, because the cauterization helps to eliminate humors, it is more beneficial.  After the two arteries have been ligated, the site should be filled with clean cotton and covered with a bandage. After healing, treat him with dry medications that promote granulation and apply poultices until he heals, God willing.
If bleeding occurs from an artery during the operation, immediately cauterize it or fill the site with lead and tightly press your hand until the bleeding stops. If you don't have those things available, place your finger on it until the blood clots.
And wash the site with very cold water until the pain subsides and tightly bandage it as needed. And what is easier and simpler than ligating the artery is cauterizing the vein with this two-bladed cauterizer after marking the locations with lead.
And lower it forcefully until it reaches the bone and the vein is cut in two places so that there is a distance between its ends because it does not adhere with this cauterization at all, and this is an image of the cauterizer.
The sharp edges are like two small sickles except that they are much less sharp than a knife because if they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them faster and the flesh would not stop bleeding quickly, so if there is some thickness in them, the heat would stick to them.
And the flesh is cut quickly, and this work is better than all works and easier and simpler, and let there be a thickness between the blades equal to the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In treating the continuous flow of hot tears from the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow is from the veins on the skull's outside, and there is no benefit in treating it with medicine, and you see the patient's face red. And he feels in his forehead a prickling like the pricking of ants, and his eyes are sunken and moist, and his eyelids have become swollen and clotted from the intensity of his tears, so that the first physicians were forced to treat them with this treatment, which they call the sword's treatment.  It consists of ordering the patient to shave off the hair on his forehead.
Then, he incisions three parallel slits in the forehead along its length. The length of each slit is about two finger widths. One slit is parallel to the length of the nose in the center of the forehead, the second slightly away from the movement of the muscle in the temple, and the third on the other side.
And he takes precaution not to cut the two arteries on the sides, and keep his hand away from the connection of the jaws. The distance from each incision should be three finger widths. Let him have ready pieces of prepared sponge or a great quantity of absorbent cloth, so that blood can be soaked up.
Then, he inserts the sharp, two-pointed scalpel whose image is shown here. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, he peels away all the skin between the incisions along with the periosteum on the bone. Then, he does the same from the middle incision to the third incision and then withdraws this scalpel. And he inserts into the first incision another tool called a sharp, one-sided, smooth scalpel. Its image is:
Its sharp side is turned upward toward the flesh attached to the skin and its smooth side toward the bone. He pushes it until it reaches the middle incision and cuts with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without reaching the outer skin.
Then, he does the same from the middle incision to the other incision. After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, he presses on the areas of clotted blood and then in each incision, he places a cotton wick and puts upon it a compress that has been soaked in syrup, oil, vinegar, or oil.
Lest a hot tumor occur, and on the third day the bandage is removed, and much water-cooling is applied with lukewarm water, then it is treated with the balm of "basilikoun" in rose grease and all that is used to treat wounds until it heals, God willing.
The Fifth Chapter.
In Treating Tears and Discharge from the Eyes Originating in the Interior of the Head.
When a person experiences much, severe, hot discharge from the eyes originating in the interior of the head and sees two small pits in his eyes, his vision is weakened and his eyelids are eroded, and the eyelashes fall out from it. And there is a severe, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment for it is this:  order the sick person to shave his forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is a severe, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing. Know from these symptoms that these substances and discharges come from many deep veins, and the best treatment for it is this:  order the sick person to shave his forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly across, and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, until it reaches the bone and remove your hand from the muscles of the temples that move when chewing until the bone is exposed and all the blood is removed with a sponge.
Then separate between the edges of the incision with clean cotton or a linen gauze, and then tighten from above with bandages and soak the bandages in syrup and oil so that a hot tumor does not occur. When you remove it and see that the hot tumor has broken, it is necessary to scrape the bone until the growth of flesh begins.
Then treat it with the drying preparation that grows flesh, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of "calphonia" and preparing a balm from them and using it to grow flesh in such wounds.
The Sixth Chapter.
In Treating What Falls Into the Ear.
All that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something like stone, such as iron or glass; or a vegetable seed like chickpeas, kernels, and the like; or something fluid like water, vinegar, and the like.
And if it is an animal, when a stone or kind of stone that does not abound in the ear falls into it, receive the ear to the sun; if you see only the stone, put something from violet or rose oil in it and then try to remove it by movement of the head or wiping with the finger. And plug the nostrils when coming up with a sneeze after placing around the ear a collar of linen or wool and stretching the ear upward; very often it comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with the fine swab whose image is [here].
If it comes out with the swab, otherwise try to remove it with a blind awl, gentle and slightly curved. If it does not come out by that, otherwise make a tube of brass and insert the end of the tube into the hole of the ear yes.
And plug what is around the tube with putty mixed with oil so that there is no way for the air except through the tube, then draw it out with your breath, a strong and repeated pull; very often it comes out by this. If it does not come out by what we have described, otherwise take from the mastic of the Nabataeans or from the hawk's-beetle clay which is used to trap birds, something small and put it in the end of the tube.
After wrapping it in tightly fitting cotton and then inserting it gently into the ear hole after the ear is dried from moisture, if it does not come out by all that we have described, then immediately resort to the incision before the hot swelling or inflammation occurs.
The incision prescription: first, dissect the patient in the occipital region and remove from him blood according to his strength; then place the patient between your hands and turn his ear upward and make a small incision at the base of the ear where its fat is, in the low position of it.
The incision should be made in a crescent shape until you reach the stone, then remove it with the tools at your disposal, and sew up the incision as quickly as possible and treat it until it heals.
As for if the thing fallen into the ear is one of the grains that swell and become inflated, try to remove it with what we have mentioned. If it does not come out, then take a delicate and fine scalpel in this manner.
And try to cut that type of grain fallen into the ear with it, but only do this if you are certain that the grain has become saturated with the moisture of the ear until it becomes many small pieces, then remove them with a blind probe or a soft brush or by suction.
As we have mentioned, it is easy to remove them. As for water that enters the ear, it is necessary to use the ear trumpet with the cone first, and after filling the ear with some cotton wool while lying on that ear containing the water, if it comes out by doing so.
Otherwise, take many smooth and fine stones along the fingers, warm them slightly with fire, and insert one of them into the ear's hole with a trumpet and lean on his leg from that side and strike it with another stone which is in the ear.
And he continues to do so with one stone after another until all the water comes out, and water may be expelled by taking a piece of reed or a feather, inserting one end into the ear, and holding the other end over fire until it burns most of its length, then replace it with another feather.
Do this several times until all the water comes out, or attract it with a tube as mentioned before with the stone. As for removing the animal that has entered into it, look if it is small in size like a flea and similar ones, treat it with what was mentioned in the section, and as for...
If the body is large and perceptible to the touch, attempt to extract it with forceps and probes. This task is easier than all that occurs in the ear.  But if extracting the worm produced in the ear, after treating it as mentioned in the section on maggots, proves ineffective.
Look into the ear in the sunlight, and if you see anything of a worm, extract it with forceps or soft probes. If nothing appears to you, take a tube of this image.
Narrow at the bottom, wide at the top, and insert the slender end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck it with a strong suction, do this several times until all the worm is extracted. If it does not yield to extraction, stop around the tube with wax as I have mentioned to you in the case of pebbles.
If it does not come out with what has been mentioned, use the maggots that I have mentioned and which the first practitioners used to kill worms, and you will find this in the section on maggots. And your pouring of ointments and medicines into the ear is done with this tool, and here is its image:.
As you see, it is made of silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, wide at the top. And if you wish, let the cannon in the belly of the tube be of firmly fitted brass, and if you wish, take a reed and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or whatever you desire of these medicines into the tube while it is in the ear, and then insert the reed with cotton from above and squeeze it with your hand, moderately squeezing until the oil is forced into the ear canal and feels.
With the patient inside, let what is poured into the ear be warmed slightly in the fire, and beware that the thing being dripped into it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear does not tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
On Treating the Obstruction of the Ear.
Some children may be born with their mothers and their ear canals unpierced.
And some people may also experience a blockage in the ear canal due to a wound or a tumor growing there, and this blockage may be deep within the ear canal, beyond what can be seen.
And some may be visible outside the opening of the ear canal, and what is deep within the ear and not visible is, in most cases, difficult to remove. And what can be seen should be placed in the sun. And look at it, and if you see the blockage visible, then open it with a delicate scalpel, the image of which will be:
Its tip will have some slight curve, limited to some extent and the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides so as not to harm the ear. If the blockage is from a tumor that has grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a gentle forceps and cut it gently with great care until all of the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, take a gentle, smooth probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage and the patient feels a heaviness of hearing, then push gently against it.
And be careful not to injure the nerve of the ear, and then insert into the ear a wick according to the width of the opening that has been dipped in Egyptian ointment. Do this for several days until you are sure that the wound will not close or take a wick and soak it in water and sprinkle powdered saffron on it.
And use it, and if you see the ear has swollen and has a hot discharge, then you should remove the wick and replace it with another wick that has been dipped in quince syrup made with rose oil until the hot swelling subsides, then treat her until she heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear and use all the treatment that stops the mentioned bleeding in the chapter on otorrhagia.
The eighth chapter.
In treating warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry, so it is necessary to grasp them with tweezers or a pincette and cut them with a scalpel from their roots, then apply powdered saffron to the site.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a fine lentil iron and treat it best after cutting and often they return if something remains from their roots, and if cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
The ninth chapter.
In treating the cold that appears on the eyelids.
Often, something resembling cold in its intensity and rigidity appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called "the cold" which is the gathering of thick moisture on the upper and lower eyelids, and the work in it is to look, if the cold spot appears prominent on the surface of the eyelid and moves in every direction, its matter is easy, so a longitudinal incision is made through it.
Then, gently remove it from every side until it is freed, then hang it with the pincette and cut it off. If you find it difficult to cut it off except after the eyelid has been pierced by the incision, that does not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, gather it with sutures and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no harm in leaving it alone because the ointment will cure and heal it. If the cold spot leans towards the inside of the eyelid towards the inner surface, turn the eyelid over and hang the cold spot with tweezers without needing to make an incision, then cut it off from every side.
If the eyelid is cut off, it does no harm, then wash the eye after cutting the membrane with salt water and treat the place with something that promotes healing until the patient recovers.
The tenth chapter.
On treating styes which appear in the eyelid.
A stye is a swelling that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it appears more often in boys. It makes their eyes heavy and they get colds in the mornings and cannot look at sunlight. Because tears speed up to them.
And so you see them leaning on their faces always and that's where they sleep, and their eyelids are under their eyebrows wet with swelling, and the stye is visible to the eye. And when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the stye between them.  The task at hand is to place the patient's head in a basin.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare from it a wick and make from that wick a circle the size that encompasses the stye from every side, then place it on top and press with your fingers from every side to gather the stye in the center of the circle.
Then make a lengthwise incision in the center of that moisture with a lancet, an incision not larger than the incision made in bloodletting. As for depth, it is necessary to cut all the skin until you reach the stye.
In most cases, the stye protrudes from the incision in the form of a white piece of fat, which you pull out with a cloth wrapped between your fingers to the outside while you turn your hand right and left until it is clean. Beware that you do not increase the incision lest the lancet pierce the eye and harm it.
If the stye does not appear to you in the first incision, it is necessary to increase the incision a little gently until the stye protrudes, then pull it out as we said. Then soak a cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area and hold it tightly. Some people crush salt.
He places it in the hollow of the incision to dissolve what remains of that moisture, then treats it until it heals. If a hot, active tumor occurs in the place, he treats it with the soothing  pessary until it heals.
The Eleventh Chapter.
In Kinds of Eye-Watering.
If hair grows in the eyelid beyond the natural course under the natural eyelids and becomes entangled, then it harms the eye and causes kinds of diseases such as continuous tearing, relaxation of the eyelids,  white film, and thickening until that becomes a cause for blinding the eye.
And eye-watering comes in four ways, either by cauterization with fire or by sharp medicine as mentioned before in the chapter on cauterization, or that it is by cutting and sewing, or by reeds as I am describing now.
It is necessary that you make the patient's head in your block and then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, otherwise, insert a needle into it with thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle through the thread from above and it should be near the hair itself, and pull the thread up with the eyelid.
And turn it with the forceps, then make a cut in the inside of the eyelid without the extra hair with the scalpel from the larger aperture to the smaller aperture, then pull out the thread and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton or linen and then sew on the eyelid with lead.
Like the shape of a leaf of the fig tree, except that it is necessary that the shape be according to what you want to raise the eyelid because people may differ in this, some need that a suitable portion be cut from the eyelid according to how much the eyelid has relaxed.
And some need less cutting of all that according to the relaxation of the eyelid, then make a cut with the scalpel on the two lines which I have taught and begin from the larger aperture to the smaller aperture and the one cut should be near the natural hair like the thickness of the forceps.
Then the surgeon enters the awl into one corner of the skin and then severs it entirely.  And then, by sewing the lips together with a needle and thread of fine wool, he closes them up. He wipes off the blood and places the remaining threads on the eyebrows with some adhesive things.
Then, the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, then he treats it. And if you wish, you leave the wound without stitching, and treat it with what dries and contracts it, for the eyelid rises when the wound closes and comes together, and stitching is better.
This face of the  tashmir I have mentioned by the ancients, except that it is a burden on the sick person and it is good work and there is no danger in it. And another face in tashmir also, which is to teach the eyelid a shape like the shape of a fig leaf as we described. Then the eyelid is raised with three awls that are spaced apart or grouped together in this manner:
Then, the remaining part of the eyelid is cut with a small pair of scissors in this manner, cutting moderately.
If you cannot hold the awls and it does not suit you, take a needle with thread and insert it into the center of the shape, and insert another thread near the larger opening and a third thread near the smaller opening, and gather the threads together moderately between your fingers, then raise your hand with them moderately.
And raise the skin that is laid upon it completely as we described, then collect the lips of the wound with stitching and treat it until it heals. If a hot tumor appears when you cut or split it, calm that tumor with qatrayt and similar soothing ointments.
And the lower eyelid may also be subject to its edges being reversed, so it is necessary to use what we mentioned of cutting, stitching, and cauterization with fire and sharp medicine, and cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is more effective and lighter on the sick person than cutting and stitching, and both cutting and stitching are more difficult.
The method of cauterization with reed is as follows: Turn the eyelid over and make an incision from the inside as described before, then create two thin reeds or pieces of wood whose length corresponds to the length of the eyelid and width less than the width of a scalpel.
And I have provided at the edges of them on both sides where the threads adhere, then bring them together gently on what remains of the eyelid and tighten the reeds on both sides tightly, leaving it for days, so that the stretched skin dies, turns black, and rots.
Until it falls of its own accord, if you delay, then cut it with pliers, then treat it until it heals.  If it adheres, the eyelid rises and the eyelashes do not fall out, and this is the form of the reeds, which you make in this shape and this measure in length and width exactly:
Chapter Twelve.
On Raising Eyelashes That Have Fallen Out Using a Needle.
If a hair or two hairs are involved in this work, take a fine needle and thread it with a thin, smooth silk thread, then bring the ends together and tie them in a very delicate knot, let the length of the thread be about a span.
Then, thread another fine thread through the needle's eye without knotting it at the end, and tie its ends if you wish. Then place the patient's head on a block near the sun so that the work is visible, since it is delicate work. Then insert the needle with the thread in the root of the superfluous hair.
And insert it into the eyelid swiftly until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread up towards the needle's eye and insert the hair, whether one, two, or three, no more.
Then pull your hand with it until the hair emerges from the eyelid with the natural hair, and if you pulled the thread and the hair did not come out with it, pull the thread down to below until the needle's eye comes out of the upper hole.
Then you put the hair back in and pull both ends, doing this until it rises to the top hole and you see it with your eye. Then you remove the stitches and thread and take them out and tighten the eyelid, leaving it tightened for two or three days until the hair settles in its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either add to it a long strand of natural hair so that it rises with it or leave it until it grows longer after a while, then raise it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On the Treatment of A Stye That Occurs in the Upper Eyelid.
The affliction that this stye subjects to is called an arnibia and this stye can be either natural or accidental, and the accidental one is from a wound closing or a cut or similar.
And the way to treat it is to cut open that closing and separate its lips and make between them a strip of cotton and tie it until it heals, and it should not be used in treating it things that dry and contract.
For if you do that, the stye returns worse than it was, so use things that soften like fenugreek and lentils boiled in water with a bit of frankincense, cottonseed oil and diachylon ointment melted with some fat mixed with it, and treat it with it.
And the goal of treating it is to try every way that it does not rejoin in the shape it was originally, and this work we have mentioned is some repair of the stye, not to return the place to its original state.
Chapter Fourteen.
On the Treatment of a Stye That Occurs in the Lower Eyelid.
This stye that is from below is the one truly called a stye and it can be natural or accidental, and the accidental one is from a wound or cut or similar.
The method of treatment for this is to take a needle with double thread, and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left opening to the right until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh, then extend the flesh upward with the needle and cut it with a wide scalpel.
If the shape of the eyelid returns to what is proper, otherwise, take a spoon and place it on the site of the incision, flipping the eyelid with it, and make two incisions on the inner side of the eyelid, so that the edges of the incisions are from the angles of the cut you made until they meet, forming an angle. Until when they meet, its shape becomes similar to this shape: .
Which is the Greek letter lambda, then remove that flesh as much as the sharp side of it is lower than what lies below the eyelid, then bring the separated parts together with two stitches that sew it with wool thread, and then treat it with what we mentioned of soothing medications and poultices until it heals.
And if the lip has been exposed from a cut or stitching or anywhere, it is necessary to make a simple incision without eyebrow hair, also as mentioned before, then separate the lips with an ointment as we mentioned.
And the saying in treating a lip whether it is from above or below, is to perform the operation according to what appears to you of the form of the lip, as it may differ greatly in shape, and the craftsman devises a way with any means available to him.
Until the shape returns to its natural form or approximates it, and the same disease tells you what is suitable for him from work and tools in most cases,.
The fifteenth chapter.
In the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may expose many people to the eyelid upper part adhering to the white of the eye, preventing its easy movement and hindering it from its natural functions, so it is necessary to insert the end of a spoon under the eyelid.
Raise it up or stretch it with a string, then cut the adhesion with a fine scalpel that does not have the sharpness of cutting knives but rather be little so as not to distress the patient when working, and it escapes the scalpel and harms the eye with its sharpness. And make the cut like you are peeling that adhesion.
And wait until when the eyelid returns to its natural shape and the whole adhesion heals, then at that time pour into the eye salty water to wash it with or drops of the black lead dissolved in water, then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a cotton thread.
And place above the eye a pad moistened with egg white, and after the third day use the ground black lead until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting off the nail and trimming the flesh of the gums.
The nail is of two kinds, either it is nervous and resembles a thin, stiff peritoneum, or it is non-nervous and resembles a white, solid moisture. If you touch it with iron or attempt to take it with a string, it will break.
And no string has been firmly embedded in it, and both types begin from the largest part to a little until it covers the eye and prevents light and movement of the eye. And the way to work in cutting it is to place the patient's head on a stone.
Then he opens his eyes and raises the eyelid with your hand, then you take the nail with a string of little bending and stretch it upwards, then take a needle and insert a hair from horse or cow hair and strong thread into it, and bend the end of the needle slightly and insert it into the middle of the nail.
And you pierce it with the needle and tie the nail with the thread, stretch it upwards, and peel off the side of the nail next to the pupil with the hair as if you are spreading it with the hair to the end, then cut the rest at the base of the nail with a fine scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural flesh of the gums.
Lest the cutting cause continual tearing, and the tendon may be sutured with a needle alone or with thread as mentioned before, then it is scraped with a smooth scalpel, the end of which is shown in this picture.
And beware that the scalpel should touch the cornea and cause its rupture, leading to a hole in the iris, then after cutting it, something of powdered salt or zanzibar  spices are put into the eye and left until another day then treated as appropriate until it heals.
If the tendon is non-nerving and it was not possible to insert a needle into it nor secure a suture, this type of tendon is often treated with medication and heals as described in the division. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
And take a gentle smooth scalpel, weak at the edge, as shown in this picture: And scrape the tendon with it gently, if you see that it is loosening and breaking apart, and the scraping only affected its surface,  put from your hour glass into the eye of zanzibar spices or red spices or powdered salt, and hold the eye shut for another day.
Then repeat the work until it is all gone, unless a hot tumor appears in the eye, then leave it and treat the hot tumor until it heals, then repeat the scraping work on it until it heals, as for the piece of flesh from the eyelid...
If the piece of flesh  causes a severe pain to the eye, then suture that piece with thread and cut some of it, do not be persistent in cutting lest it cause tearing. Then instill into the  eye's cavity red or zanzibar spices until it heals.
The Seventeenth Chapter.
On cutting the excrescence and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
Red flesh may grow in the eyes of some people, layered upon each other until it covers the pupil or approaches it, or overflows onto the eyelids, and perhaps the eyelids turn outward, resembling a blooming rose.
The operation here is to place the patient's head in a stone, then open his eyes and take all that red flesh with forceps appropriate for it.
Or you grasp it with a scalpel or forceps, then cut the first flesh until all of it is gone by cutting, and be cautious of the eye so that it will not be harmed during the operation. The cutting for him is either with the knife we described in cutting the fingernail or with a small pair of scissors. Like those that grasp the threads as they come, whose image is shown in the chapter following this one.
And if the cutting is finished and all of the redness has gone, fill the eye with powdered salt or drip into it a few drops of red ointment or similar medicinal substances that are effective. And place on the eye from outside a cotton ball dipped in egg white to prevent inflammation and hot swelling.
And if any redness remains and blood overcomes you and you fear hot inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with what soothes inflammation, then return to the operation until it heals, similarly do with the excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
Except that it is necessary for you to avoid treating every weak eye by nature or by the operation with iron, and do not put your hand into anything of these operations until you see that that organ is capable of it.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
In Pulling Threads from the Eye.
Threads are red veins that weave on the eye, which prevents sight, and weakens the eye with the passing of days. So first it is necessary for you to look, if the eye in which the threads are strong and there was no other illness except the threads.
Then, the operation is that you order the patient to place his head in a stone, then suspend those veins with one or two forceps according to your skill. And the forceps are gentle in bending in this image:
Or it may be two piles joined together in one body in this way:.
Then seize with a fine pair of scissors those veins gently and wipe the blood every now and then until you see the eye that the veins have gone from it and the bleeding has stopped, and be careful of the eye, do not harm it with the points of the scissors, and let your work be half the day facing the sun.
And be very careful in your work so that you do not cut any other veins, and when you are finished, put in the eye the green or red spatula to eat with its sharpness what remains of the cords, if you cannot take all of it in that hour, then staunch the eye with something that soothes the pain of the eye.
And leave it for days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling is safe, then repeat the work on her in this same way until this image of the scissors' pain departs from it.
And leave it [and let the head of the patient be on the student's stone and the other dries the tear and observes the sight in front of the eye and its end and you] the original in the work is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openers grasped by a student.
Sharp and let the head of the patient be on the student's stone and the other dries the tear and observes the sight in front of the eye and its end and you, by choice, begin to cut from the top of the eye or from the bottom, and it is best to dry two piles in the upper part of the eyebrow, one at the end of the crown and the other at the beginning.
And hold them between the middle finger and index finger, then attach pins from below opposite them, then hold them between the ring finger and little finger, and move your hand slightly until you see all the cords have risen, cut from the smaller side with scissors if it is possible, and with a fine scalpel if not.
And drill the pus through a needle and stop all the cords until it rises on the mound, and do not cut the discharge for if the tear and blood become mixed (confusion) on you.
Then cut with the scissors what exceeds the crown from above and below until only the root remains in the horns. If you can raise it from the horns, scrape it off with a blunt instrument; this is better. And it may be difficult in some people.
And if you cut it, it should rest for days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling calms, then resume work on it.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
In Returning the Feather to the Nose.
Doctors call a feather a  "nasara," if treated with the cautery or a sharp, burning drug as described above and it does not heal, there is no remedy except to split open the swelling when ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed. And if, when the bone is exposed, you see decay or blackness in it, remove it with an instrument, the image of which is:
It is called a "head chisel," it is made from Indian iron, and its head is round like a grain of wheat.  A fine engraving of the scraper or scalpel has been engraved on it. You place it on the site of the decay in the bone then turn it between your fingers while you squeeze your hand slightly until you feel that the decay has been removed.
Do this several times, then cover the place with drying, constricting medicines. If the place closes up and flesh grows in it, and the flow ceases and lasts for forty days without recurrence or irritation,
Then know that it is healed. Otherwise, there is no remedy except to return the "nasara" to the hole in the nose according to this method: reveal the bone again with iron or a sharp drug. If the bone is exposed, take a moistened one according to this image:
Its tip is triangular and its shaft conical, drawn toward the end as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, near the nostril and slightly move your hand away from the eye, then turn your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
If he finds the wind coming out of it when he places his hand over his nose, then the place is tightened with astringent and drying medicines as I have taught you. And if the flesh grows and the place becomes solid, then the material that was pointing outwards returns to the nose, so that it will be easier for the sick person, God willing.
Chapter Twenty
On Returning a Protruding Eye.
If the eye protrudes with all of its parts and no harm or deficiency occurred in the vision, it is necessary to make the patient comfortable then detach it from the skull and place a covering on the back of the head without any constraint and apply a fine poultice.
Then place on the eye a poultice made of acacia, frankincense, myrrh, and cinnabar, and then tie the eye from above on the bandage with many layers of  bandages. The recipe for the bandages is to take soft linen rags and fold each one four times, making many.
Then place them one on top of the other to the extent the eye needs, and then tie it with a firm hard cloth tightly. Then replace the covering with the poultice without any constraint, then remove the covering and leave the eye tied for a day and night.
If the bandage relaxes during that time, it is necessary to tighten it again. Then remove the bandage after a day and night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise, repeat the bandage, layers, tightening, and covering until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One
On Cutting a Pimple
If a pimple appears in the skin layer, then it protrudes and bursts outside the eyelids like a grape, and deforms the appearance of the human being because of it, and you want to cut it, then the work done on it is as I describe.
And it is that you insert a needle into the base of the pimple from below to above, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the side of the eye and pass it through, leaving the first needle as it is.
Then the place of the bend of the doubled thread is cut and some lint around the needle is tied to it and tightened well. Then the needle is withdrawn and on the eye is placed a cotton swab moistened with albumen and left until the threads and lint fall off, then the eye is treated with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, and the lint may be tightened from every side with a circle of gauze until the eye becomes more taut well. Then it is pressed with a fine scalpel reaching to the depth of the eye so that the albuminous moisture flows out and the eye descends and dries on its place then tightened until it heals.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In Treating Cataracts.
This ailment called cataract is a collection that comes together in the eye resembling flowing water but not possessing it, and the way to work on it is that you make the patient sit on an upright stool, then take his head with your hands from both sides.
And move him until you see the collection descend to the lower part with your eye, then it becomes fixed and does not move, and light emanates and the patient sees things as he used to see them. If it does not descend to the lower part, we know that it is water. And if it does not appear to descend in what we have mentioned.
Otherwise, make the patient sit in front of you, then take a fine scalpel and slightly cut above the cornea where the connection with the cornea at the circumference meets until the material comes out.
If it comes out, pour into the eye hot water that has been mixed with honey or water that has been boiled with honey and then treat with all other treatments until it heals.
The Twenty-Third Chapter.
We have mentioned the types of water in the classification and which one is suitable for treatment with a detailed explanation, so take it from there on sound health. Then it is necessary that you make the patient sit in front of you upright facing the light near the sun.
Bind the eye soundly and tightly, then raise its eyelid with your left hand if the eye with the water is the left eye, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then place the tip of the probe near the circle with a thick rod in the same white of the eye from the smaller side and then push the probe forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel that the probe has reached something empty.
It should reach a depth equal to the distance from the pupil to the end of the black, which is the circle of the eye, for you see the copper in the same way the eye sees because of the transparency of the cornea; then, turn the probe upward to the place where the water is.
Then press it down repeatedly; if the water descends from its place, then the patient sees what has opened before his eyes from his time and the probe in his eye, and he will rest a little; if the water rises, push it down again without removing the probe.
If it settles and does not rise, then gently remove the probe while you slightly loosen your grip on it; then dissolve a little bit of pure salt in the water and wash the eye from inside; then place a compress or wrung-out wool outside the entire eye.
Anoint it with rose water and egg white and bind the eye together; as for us and our people, we place on top of it cumin that has been pounded with egg white; if the probe does not enter the eye because of its hardness, since some people have eyes that are very hard. Where you should take the forceps called "brid," which has this image: .
Pierce it with the trepan only, and do not be excessive in the piercing, but rather that you carefully approach the area of the skull, then introduce the borer as mentioned before. When you have finished your work, prepare the sick man lying down firmly, where he will sleep on his back in a dark room.
And he refrains from all movement and coughing, and you make his food what softens his nature, and he does not move his head right or left at all. And the bandage remains on him for three days, then you remove it in that dark room, and try his sight.
And you show him things, then return the bandage for seven days, and it is not right to do that during treatment or after removing the borer from its time, but rather you must avoid that before water rises quickly with sharp vision.
For if a hot swelling appears, it is necessary to remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat it with what soothes the swelling until when it subsides, then you release his sight and put on his face a veil that trains his vision under it for days while he is in that dark room, then.
He goes out of the house gradually and deals with his affairs, and know that the borer is indispensable to the learner without observation several times, then he proceeds to work. It has reached me about some Iraqis that they say he makes a borer in Iraq that absorbs water.
