The Fifth and Seventy-Second Chapter.
In teaching about the afterbirth, how to treat living ones that have come out in an unnatural way.
It is necessary for the midwife to know first the natural form of birth, for its signs: if you see the woman straining downwards and yearning to smell the air and her pain from labor eases.
And the fetus emerges quickly, know that this birth is in a natural way and its exit is on its head, with the placenta beside it or suspended from its umbilical cord, so if you see these signs, it is necessary to massage her abdomen so that the fetus comes out quickly.
For if it descends on its head, the placenta descends with it and cleanses from those impurities a good cleansing, and other than this birth, it is unnatural and blameworthy because the fetus may come out on its feet, and its hands come out before its head and feet.
And one hand or one foot, or it emerges with its head and hand, or emerges curled up, and perhaps rolls onto its back, and so on from the forms that are blameworthy, it is necessary for the midwife to be skillful and gentle with all of these things and to beware of mistakes.
And I explain every form of it and how the maneuver is done in it so that you may deduce it and arrive at it, if the fetus emerges headfirst in the natural manner and the woman is burdened by it and finds childbirth difficult, and I see her strength has weakened, then I make her sit on a stool and order women to hold her steady.
And I medicate her womb with cooked fenugreek and wet poultices, then the midwife inserts between her fingers a small scalpel and cuts the placenta open or tears it with her fingernail until the moisture within it flows out, and she squeezes the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, it is necessary to inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with sheep tallow, then command her after the injection to strain and sneeze, holding her mouth and nose shut, for the fetus will emerge within an hour. If the baby's hand emerges first, it is necessary to gently push it back in little by little.
If it does not come to entering, then place the woman on a platform and raise her legs above, then rock the platform back and forth on the ground and the woman holds on so she does not fall during the rocking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a strip of cloth and pull them gently, for it will come out. Exit of the fetus on its feet, if the fetus comes out on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards then turn the fetus gradually until it is straight, then take one of its legs and extend it little by little.
If they emerge to the hips, order her to strain and sneeze with a kands, for it will come out. If it does not come out as we described, or else turn the fetus back little by little until it becomes in its natural form, then it will come out smoothly at that time. If it refrains from all that we have described.
Take a portion of the saliva of the white henbane, and the saliva of the linseed, and butter of sheirj, and gum dissolved, and beat them all in the mortar with good beating, then apply it to the vagina and the lower abdomen, then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the level of the womb.
If you see that it has become loose below, make for her a sheet of marsh weed and she should hold it. If she holds the sheet for an hour, then seat her on the stool, then have her cough and plug her mouth and nose, and squeeze her lower abdomen gently, for the fetus will come out in an hour, coming out on his knees and hands.
If it comes out in this manner, gently insert her hands a little at a time, then have the woman lie on her back and let her legs hang down while she is stretched out on a bed, and hold her hands together then squeeze lightly above her abdomen. Then bring out what is under her legs from the cushions until she is tilted downwards, if the fetus does not come out, take both of her legs and move them with a strong movement, then squeeze above the womb gently until the fetus ascends upwards, then the midwife enters her hand and smooths the fetus little by little.
And order the woman to strain until the fetus comes out, the fetus coming out with one hand outstretched and reaching forward, hold onto her hand if you can pull it back, if she cannot pull it back then put the woman up and make her walk, if she cannot walk then have her lie down.
Place her on the bed and shake her legs violently, then use the pessary which was described from saliva, if she has the contractions, then seat her on a stool, and then act in pulling back the hand and smoothing the fetus to its natural shape, have the woman cough and order her to strain until it comes out.
The fetus emerges on its buttocks with outstretched arms, and its face is towards the mother's back. The midwife must grasp the fetus’ hand and turn it gradually little by little gently until it is aligned, then apply to her abdomen what we have described of fat and ointments.
Then she should move it gradually little by little. If she does so, seat her on the chair and order her to incline to the right side, then tell her to fidget and tickle her with a reed, for this will bring him out, the fetus emerging sideways.
If he descends into the vaginal opening in this manner with the placenta, split the placenta with your fingernail until it is free of moisture. If it clings to the fetus's face and neck, cut it from the navel so that he does not choke and die. Then push the fetus inside until you position him as appropriate.
And order the woman to fidget several times, for this will bring him out easily. As for twins or many fetuses, know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four, but this is rare.  As for five, it is something outside of nature and they do not live at all.
And they are surrounded by and if they are many, a single placenta. They are separated by a partition between them, attached to the navel of each one.  They may emerge as one fetus emerges, with an easy and natural birth, or they may emerge in a manner described previously.
If the birth of some is difficult and becomes like what we have said from unnatural shapes, treat him as we have described.  Let the midwife be gentle and do what she does with gentleness, patience, and ingenuity, using every means at her disposal that leads to safety.
Mention what is imagined in the womb of fetuses that are aborted, sometimes one and two and three and four and five and six and seven and more than ten. It has reached me that a woman miscarried seven and another fifteen, all of them imagined.
With the knowledge of the All-Wise and Exalted, this all should be in the science of the midwife, for perhaps such a thing may happen to her one day, so she may meet it with what is appropriate.
Chapter sixty-seven.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
If you treated the dead fetus with what I mentioned in the classification of treatment and it did not come out, and you were compelled to work with your hand, then it is necessary that you look. If the woman is healthy in strength and there is nothing from the symptoms that one fears for her from damage, then it is necessary that the woman lie on a bed on her back.
And her head is inclined downwards and her legs are raised, and a servant holds her from both sides or ties her to the bed so that her body does not be pulled when the fetus is delivered.
Then he moistens the mouth of her womb with the wet ointments, along with the saliva of the catmint, and the fennel, and the flaxseed, then the midwife anoints her hand with these ointments and saliva, then she puts it gently into the womb, then she seeks a place for it to insert the forceps from the fetus, and the suitable places for that.
Look, if the fetus is positioned so that it descends on its head, then insert the forceps into its eyes or forehead or mouth or chin or under its beard or throat or in the places close to the ribs or under the shoulder blades.
As for if its descent is on its legs, then in the groin and the middle ribs, it is necessary that you hold the forceps with your right hand and make its bend between the fingers of your left hand, and insert the hand gently and insert a forceps into some of those places we mentioned.
Until you reach something empty, then insert another forceps before it, or a third if needed to be the pulling equal and not lean to one side, then extend a straight pull and it is not only on a straight line but moves in every direction.
It is easy to extract it, as it is done with a tooth when it is extracted, and it is necessary in this interval that the cord be relaxed. If something is obstructed from it, it is necessary that the midwife lubricate some fingers with fat and insert them from one side to guide what is obstructed, if part of the fetus comes out, then the forceps are moved to other places.
She is slightly raised doing this until the entire fetus comes out, if a hand came out before the others and could not be returned because of its compression, it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it and then pull it until when all of it comes out cut it.
Whether in the wrist or in the elbow and so on, it is necessary to do the same with the other hand and in the legs, if the head of the fetus is large and there is pressure on it when exiting or if there is water collected in its head, it is necessary to insert between the fingers a sharp scalpel.
And it is cut with it the head so that the water comes out or it is lanced with the instrument called the  "mashdak," which comes with a picture of all other instruments in the chapter after this one.
Similarly, this is done if the fetus is large headed, of course, it is necessary to cut the skull or lance it with the mashdak as we mentioned, and then the bones are removed with forceps, if the head comes out and compresses at the clavicle, let it be cut until the moisture in the chest flows out.
For then the chest joins, if the chest does not join and it is not possible to come out, cut with all your might, if the lower abdomen is a hard tumor or there is a hernia in it, it is necessary to flatten it until the moisture seeps out of it.
If the fetus is on its legs, then pulling it is easy and moving it to the mouth of the womb is simple, if it compresses at the abdomen or chest, it is necessary to pull it with a cloth wrapped around your hand and cut the abdomen or chest until what is in them seeps out.
If all the organs are extracted and the head is returned but remains trapped, let the midwife insert her left hand. If the mouth of the womb is open, she inserts her hand into the neck of the womb and seeks the head with it, pulling it to the mouth of the womb with her fingers, then she inserts a cord or two cords into it and pulls it out with them.
If the mouth of the womb has closed up due to a hot swelling, it is not proper to force it open, but rather at that time it is necessary to use applications of moist, fatty substances and to apply compresses and sit in water that softens and moistens.
As for if the fetus is on its side, if it can be set straight, use what was mentioned for a living fetus. If that is not possible, cut it and remove it, and it is necessary to leave nothing of the placenta inside at all. When you have finished treatment, apply what was mentioned from treatment for hot swellings that appear in the wombs.
If bleeding occurs, she should descend into constricting waters and apply the rest of the treatment. I have seen a woman who had become pregnant, then the fetus died in her womb, and she became pregnant with it again.
Then the other fetus also died. After a long time, she had a swelling in her womb that swelled until it opened and began to discharge pus. I was called to treat her, and I treated her for a long time, but the wound did not close. So I put on it some strong ointments that draw.
A bone came out of the place, and after a few days another bone came out. I was amazed at this because the abdomen is not a place where there are bones. I estimated that it was from the bones of the dead fetus, so I searched the wound and removed many bones from the head of the fetus.
I did not stop doing this until I removed many bones from her, and the woman is in the best of health. She has lived like that for a while, pus coming out of the place, but I have brought here this rare case.
For therein is knowledge and assistance for what the physician-maker attempts with his hand in treatment.
The Seventh and Seventy Chapter.

An image of a screw used to open the mouth of the womb:
This clamp, which is made for books as well, has two screws at its wooden ends, except that this screw should be more gentle than the clamp and should be either from ebony or boxwood and the width of each wood should be like the thickness of two fingers.
And their curves are towards a finger and their length is a palm and a half, and in the middle of the two woods there are two grooves from the same type of wood that were tightened within them; their length should be half a palm or a little more and their width should be like a finger or a little more. And these are the grooves.
These are the ones that enter into the mouth of the womb to open it with them when you operate the screws.  An image of another instrument similar to this, more gentle and lighter:
It is made from ebony or boxwood in the shape of a kalam, except that it has two grooves at its end as you see; the length of each groove is about a palm and their width is like a finger. If you want to open the womb with it:
Then you make the woman sit on a lowered bed, her legs spread apart between her thighs, and then you insert these two grooves, pressed together, into the mouth of the womb while you hold the end of the instrument below between her hips, and then open your hand with the instrument as you do with kalam whether to the extent that you want to open the womb. Chapter Eighty-Seventh.
In extracting the placenta.
If the placenta remains after childbirth, it is necessary to order the midwife to hold herself back and then she should give her a  kandus  to blow and pull on your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, otherwise take a cup.
And pierce a hole in the lid and put in herbs that open the mouth of the womb like  fodnj, sizzab, dill, chamomile, asafoetida, sleekha, and centaury. All these medicines or some of them are immersed in water and then placed on fire. Then you place a silver tube on the hole in the lid and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
And hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, then give her a  kandus  as we said before, and the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to immerse her left hand in the fat of sheep or the saliva of the cat.
Then she puts it in the vulva and searches for the placenta with it. If she finds it, she grabs it and pulls it out little by little. If it is stuck deep in the womb, then put your hand as we described before until you find the placenta, then pull it out little by little on a cushion of cloth.
Lest the uterus should fall when pulled with great force, it is necessary to move it gently to the sides, right and left. Then increase the amount of pulling, for then it will respond and get rid of adhesions. If the mouth of the uterus is closed, we have described treatment by cauterization and treatment with herbs and applications.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of force upon her in pulling again, but rather it is necessary to tie what has come out from it to the mouth of the uterus to the woman’s thigh and then inject it with the four-part ointment for it will soften after days.
And it will dissolve and come out, except that if it has become soft, a foul smell ascends from it to the stomach and head and this harms the patient, so it is necessary to use the appropriate barley meal.
Therefore, and because some of the ancients tried fumigation with chaff and dried figs, like the instrument by which a woman is fumigated when she has retained her menses or placenta, and so on:
It is made of lead resembling a funnel, or it is made of copper with the narrow end placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire and incense is placed on charcoal; hold it until that incense goes and replace it with other incense.
Chapter seventy-nine.
In the treatment of the imperforate anus.
Many infants are born with their anuses imperforate, obstructed by a thin peritoneum; therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to pierce that peritoneum with her finger or else to cut it with a sharp scalpel, and she must be cautious not to touch the muscle.
Then she places a cloth soaked in the syrup and oil on it and treats it with ointments until it heals. If she fears that it will close again, place a lead tube in the hole for many days and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes a lesion may be closed by means of a wound or tumor, and in this case it is necessary to cut open that lesion and then treat it with what has been mentioned from the treatment and its components.
The Eighty-eighth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Fistulas That Occur Below.
Fistulas that occur below are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus from outside or in the space from one of the sides, and the fistula is one or more.
And if that complication opens and a white watery fluid or thin pus flows from it, and some of these fistulas may penetrate into the bladder and intestines, and they may be non-penetrating; the penetrating ones can be known by what comes out from them of feces and air when using the stool.
And perhaps worms may come out of it, and some of them may be fistulas if they are in the space penetrating into the bladder and urethra, and they may also be penetrating into the hip joint and coccyx, and what is known as a fistula penetrating into the anus is also from the non-penetrating.
That you enter your index finger into the anus and insert a thin probe made of copper or iron into the fistula from within if there is no swelling in it, and if there is swelling, insert a thin lead probe or a hair from horse hairs.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger, and if you do not feel it at all and nothing comes out of the hole from feces or air or worms as we said, then know that it is non-penetrating, so proceed to treatment.
And if the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or urethra, its evidence is the flow of urine from it and its refusal to adhere to the place with medicines, as for if it is penetrating into the hip joint or coccyx, its sign is reaching the probe there.
If there is no penetration to the bone, and pain exists in that direction and pus constantly exits from it, and if it does not respond to treatment nor close with ointment, and all these penetrating fistulas are like this, then there is absolutely no cure for them.
And treatment for them is a futile effort for those who attempt them from among the ignorant physicians, but as for the ones that are not penetrating and not chronic, one hopes for healing by iron as I have described and as has been tried, which is to place the patient before you on his back.
And the legs are moved upward and the thighs are slanted towards his belly, then a copper probe or lead one if it is in the fistula is inserted until you know where the probe ends. If the patient feels it near the rectum, then it is necessary to insert your index finger into the rectum.
If you feel in your finger that the probe has penetrated by itself, exposed without feeling between your finger and it any roughness or flesh, then know for sure that it is penetrating, so do not tire yourself out in it for there is no cure from it as we have said.
And they have said that it heals in some people rarely, and of the treatment that one hopes for benefit from, is to heat a thin iron plate according to the size of the fistula as previously mentioned and insert it protected into the fistula until it reaches near the rectum, then return it two or three times.
Until you know that all those excess flesh growths that resemble a bird feather tube have burned, then treat it with a poultice moistened in tallow until those burned growths exit, then treat it with cohesive ointments if he heals.
And otherwise, it does not heal without it from treatment ever, but as for if you insert the probe and it did not penetrate to your finger in the rectum, and there was a thick veil between them of flesh or peritoneum, and you saw the fistula below the skin surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula. And you pass through the incision with the probe while it is in the pliers until you reach the incision where the end of the probe reaches, and the probe is removed and falls out, then you clean those fleshy parts that resemble a bird’s feather tube and nothing remains from them.
Then you treat it with healing ointments until it heals, and if blood overcomes you and hinders your work to cut those fleshy parts, the best thing you can do and the quickest benefit for that is cauterization by fire or with a sharp medicine because cauterization by fire brings together two good states: burning those excess fleshy parts.
And cutting the blood and drying up the moisture, then you treat it with a swab moistened in butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the place pus and those burned fleshy parts come out in the pus, then treat the wound then with healing ointments suitable for hardening flesh.
And these are the medicines that have contraction and drying, so if it is closed, it has healed and there is no return, if the pliers ended in the depth of the buttock and far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the buttocks and search in it.
If you felt the probe and between you and it a curtain of peritoneum or flesh, and it was close to breaking through, there is no work in it except on the path of hope and longing, that is to say, use one of three ways: either mold it as we have said.
Or you cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert a swab and treat from near, do not overdo the cutting lest you cut the muscle surrounding the buttocks, causing an ailment where feces come out without volition, then treat it with what we have mentioned, perhaps it heals as we said.
Or you penetrate that curtain and pierce it after cutting it near the buttocks as we said, either with the probe or with another tool sharp-pointed, then clean all those fleshy parts in the incision to where you were able to investigate.
Then he sutured the entire wound, including the incision and upper opening, until it was closed, leaving the drain open inside the seat so that it would be easier for the patient. And sometimes, the drain can be removed in this manner.
And if he inserted the probe into the drain and it was on one side of the seat near the surface of the body with the skin and the ring of the seat, then take a probe that is perforated at the end like an awl in this way.
And he inserted a thread that is open from five threads or thereabouts into it, then he inserted the probe with the thread into the drain until it reached its bottom. If it was expanded at the edge of the seat from inside near.
Then he brought the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the seat and bringing out the end of the thread, and gather both ends together and tighten them, leaving it for a day or two.  And whenever the thread is cut in the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads is severed and falls off, then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
And if the mouth of the drain is not expanded, expand it as much as you can except that it should be deep, for you must not do that because of the muscle so that you do not cut it, then make it as we mentioned until it heals.
Image of the awl that is used to cut the drains, its point is very sharp and the other side is not sharp so that it does not cut what it does not need as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
In treating hemorrhoids that bleed and cut them, and treating anal fissures.
Hemorrhoids are in two types, either they are inside the seat resembling reddish bulges or like grapes and they are small and large and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple and may occur outside the anus, on its edges. However, those that are outside the anus are usually less moist and exude a yellowish fluid or a little blood constantly flowing. They are on the color of the body.
And the treatment for what is inside the anus is to order the sick person to defecate and make a cleansing until the anus protrudes and the warts appear before you. Then hasten, attach them with tweezers or grasp them with your finger, then cut them at their roots. If tweezers do not hold in them because of their moisture and relaxation.
So scrape them with a rough piece of cloth or a piece of leached bread, or pull them out with your fingers and then cut them. Then put some sharp points on them after cutting to do the work of cauterization, or cauterize them as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until it heals. If the anus does not respond to coming out, inject the patient with an injection containing a little leeches’ liquid to wash what is in the anus and encourage its exit quickly when the patient cleanses himself.
And it is that you take it with your fingernail or hang it with a string and cut it, then treat it as we have mentioned until it heals, and whoever dislikes cutting with iron, it is necessary to use its thread in this way: take an unspun thread and insert it into a needle.
Then you pull the wart upwards and insert it with the needle at its base from the other side, and wrap the ends of the thread below the needle as it is crossed, and tighten the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this for all warts, and leave one without threading it.
So that the blood drains from it, then you place a cloth soaked in rose grease or qayrouti on the seat and place it on the seat and order the patient to rest, then leave him until it falls. If the warts fall, treat them with ointments and other treatment until they heal.
As for the treatment of fissures, it is often exposed from the feces and retention of stool, if it is delayed and no medicine benefits in it, then it is necessary to remove it with a scalpel or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper crust that prevents it from healing disappears.
Then treat it until it heals properly, and if it does not heal, then re-treat it with a sharper edge than the first until it bleeds well and peels off, then treat it and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Second.
On the Treatment of Ingrown and Non-Ingrown Nails, Dry Warts, and  Livers Spots.
The nail is a round knot the color of the body, resembling a nailhead, which appears on all parts of the body, especially the soles of the feet and fingers, and causes pain when walking.
Then it is necessary to split what surrounds the nail and grasp it with a tweezers or string and pull it from its root, then treat it. And if you wish, cauterize it as mentioned in its chapter. As for the dry warts, each one of them is a small, rough, protruding round wart on the surface of the body.
It is often found in the hands of boys, and its treatment is to extend the foreskin and cut it or cauterize it with silk thread or hair until it falls off. And if you wish, burn it as mentioned before with fire or with a sharp medicine.
And warts, both inverted and non-inverted, can be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of moles. As for the mole, it is also a small, hard bump on the surface of the body that goes deep inside. If cold affects it, you feel something similar to the sting of an ant.
It is found on the whole body, and most often in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from an ostrich feather or a strong eagle feather and place that tube on the mole until it surrounds the mole in a circle. Then you turn your hand with the tube until you split what is around the mole.
And push it into the depth of the flesh and the mole is removed from its root. And if you wish, do this with a tube of copper or iron in this way:
The top of the tube should be to the point, solid and open so that it is easy for the fingers to hold it and control it. And if you wish, cauterize the mole as mentioned before in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
In the shapes of instruments used for injections in cases of hemorrhoids, diarrhea and colic.
The syringe can be made of silver, porcelain, or hollow or beaten copper. And from this instrument, small and large ones can be made according to those who use them. So the ones used to treat young children, whose buttocks are narrow or painful, their syringes should be very gentle. The image of a large syringe:
Its upper part which attaches the tube is wide in this way, it has a barrier where the tube is attached as you see, and its lower end which enters the anus is solid and very smooth to the point. On one side, there are two holes, and on the other side, there is one hole.
The diameter of the hole should be equal to or slightly thicker than the thickness of the reed, and the tube in which the medicine is placed should be made from the bladder of an animal or the intestine of a lamb, shaped like a small plate, and its diameter should be one finger's width.
And it is pierced with many holes as it revolves, and the distance between each hole should be the thickness of a finger, then a thick thread of doubled yarn, ten threads or so, is inserted into those holes and the tube is gathered like a plate. So if medicine is placed in it, the syringe's head is tightened between the stones with a firm grip, then it is injected. And this is a picture of the tube and the hole:.
And the way to inject it is to fill the intestine or bladder with medicine while it is warmed on a fire according to the patient's possibility, and beware that it is not too hot or too cold, then the patient lies on his back and raises his legs.
And a piece of cloth or blanket is placed under him, then the tip of the syringe is smeared with butter or egg white or licorice saliva or something similar, and it is gently inserted into the anus, then the servant of the syringe takes it in both hands and squeezes it hard until all that is in the syringe is emptied into the intestines.
Then the syringe is removed and the patient's legs are raised to the wall, if the medicine encourages him to go, then he should hold on as much as he can, and if he sleeps with it all night, it was more effective in purging. If he was injected for diarrhea and his discharge was quickened, then the injection should be repeated until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, then the syringe should be very gentle and smoother than the syringes used for children, and this is a picture of a gentle syringe with its skin tightened on it:.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification the treatment of things resembling boils, and here I am discussing their treatment by hand and medicine in detail. I say that wounds differ according to the thing that is wounded and according to the place where the wound occurs.
And the things that cause wounds are numerous, like a stone strike or a cut from a sword or knife or a spear thrust or a stick or an arrow and similar things in great number.
And as for wounds according to the places in the body, like a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and rule for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds especially because complex ones will be mentioned in the first chapters of this book.
So I say, when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no broken bone if it was from a stone strike or similar, and it had only split the skin and the wound was large and I feared for the patient the occurrence of hot swelling, then act quickly and drain the cephalalgia in place. And do not delay that and bring out from the blood to the extent of its strength if nothing prevents bleeding, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is easy, let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded area.
And warn the patient against fullness and drinking and heavy foods, if something prevents him from bleeding, then let him reduce his food and avoid meat and drinks, and apply to the wound if it has a hot swelling cotton soaked in rose fat alone or with the drink containing contraction.
And if the hot swelling has subsided, apply to the wound if it is soft with blood and the air did not change it, the one that I will describe for you after a while, but if it has changed it, apply some ointments to it.
Until it extends the pus and then treat it until it heals, if the wound is large and from a sword cut or similar, and its edges did not meet with the surrounding tissue, collect them by stitching as I have described in suturing abdominal wounds.
If the skin was exposed to bone and clung but its holding was only in a slight adhesion, cut it then treat the wound with medicines that strengthen it to form firm flesh instead of skin, and order the patient to eat foods containing strength like heads, tail meat, etc.
If there was rot in the skin and it did not adhere to bone, cut out all the rotten parts and throw them away then treat the wound, if there was an artery or vein in these wounds that bled and the blood did not stop with medicines then search for the artery.
If you hit it and it did not stop bleeding, then cut it off with a scalpel and tie it up, and if necessity demanded and what was mentioned was ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stopped. If these wounds were small and simple, order that they are easy and simple to treat by simply applying this powder made from its blood to them.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is that a part of frankincense and two parts of sulfur and three parts of slaked or unslaked lime are taken, all crushed together and sifted, then stuffed into the wound and tightened tightly until it adheres to a good seal.
It clots with blood then tighten it from above with the surrounding tissue and leave it undisturbed as long as it does not swell, because the medicine does not come off of it until it heals, and the lime alone may do this if you did not have frankincense and sulfur.
And this can also be done with a poultice in many large wounds if it was skillfully placed on them and tightened, and the wounds were fresh with their blood as long as the air did not change it, but if the air had changed it some, especially if it was during summer.
There is no meaning in treating this kind of abscess with what we have mentioned, as for putting some prepared ointments on it or putting a poultice of barley flour made with water and honey on it until the pus drains out, then treat it with all other treatments until it heals.
But if a fracture occurs in the bone with the wound and it is slight, then pull it together with a splint, and I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures in what comes after, in surgery on the neck, if the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference in the treatment between it and a wound on the head if it is simple.
As for if a nerve from the neck or an artery has been cut, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with what causes strong compression like cinchona bark and turmeric because they harm the nerve and tighten it.
And nothing cold should be put on the wound at all because the essence of the nerve is cold and its connection to the brain which is the noblest of organs, and what treats it with should be soft medicines like rose petals washed with fresh water several times moistened with oil or rose ointment and mulberry.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all the minerals on this condition and wet ointments, but if the wound is large, use suturing or bring its edges together with pads then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has a hollow and there is a gathering of pus at its bottom, then empty it in the lowest place in it, if an artery in the wound has been cut and its blood is flowing, then cut it or tie it or cauterize it if necessity demands that.
If the wound has cut some or all of the uvula, and the parts have remained whole, then gather the edges of the skin with suturing on the base of the uvula, do not touch the uvula but smooth it and return it to its natural shape then tighten it tightly and leave it for a few days.
Then he treats it with ointments containing contraction and drying until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, then open it. If there are fragments of bone inside, extract all of them or as much as possible. If you cannot remove them all, leave them until the wound dries up and heals, and it will be easier to remove them.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described. If a bone hardens and becomes calloused in the wound, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, on the surgery of the chest and between the shoulders.
If it is a wound from a spear or sword and you see that it has an opening, look. If air comes out when the patient breathes, know that it is a lethal wound. If it does not have an opening and is fresh with blood, do not put any powder in it from the first moment nor tighten it so that the blood may not be trapped in its depth.
It will lead to the heart and kill the patient, but put a contracting ointment in it and apply it twice during the day. If you do not have ointment, put a cotton ball moistened in the mouth of the wound to absorb what comes out of it. And make the patient sleep on the wound.
Let it drain what gathers in it. If three days or more have passed for the wound and there is no twitching or fluttering of the heart, nor shortness of breath in the patient, and you see that his other conditions are good, know that the wound is healthy. Treat it with cauterization and all other treatments until it heals.
If it is difficult to see and the pus continues constantly, know that it has become calloused. Treat it according to its chapter. The patient should sleep on the wound so that it drains what gathers in it. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with what was previously mentioned about suturing.
If it is large or with powder, if it is small. If it has affected the bone and cut fragments of it, open the wound and immediately extract those fragments. If they are clean, if not, leave them until...
The wound is cleansed, for it facilitates its extraction, and as for the rest of the wounds that occur in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the rule mentioned.
Chapter Fifty-Eighth.
On Surgeries of the Abdomen and Protruding Intestines and Their Suturing
The rupture that occurs in the abdomen may be large, or small, or medium; the large one may allow intestines or several intestines to protrude; and its re-insertion and suturing are more difficult and harder.
And the small rupture may also be difficult from another aspect, so its return is harder; and if the intestine is not immediately put back in its place within an hour, it swells and thickens, making its insertion difficult; therefore, the best of ruptures is the medium one because its return is not as difficult as in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways; the two ordinary stitches are suitable for suturing the abdomen and all other bodily wounds, and the other two special stitches apply to all surgeries except that they are more specific for suturing the abdomen.
And I describe them to you one by one with explanation and description; I say that if the wound is small and something of the intestine protrudes from it and its return is difficult, this is due to one of two reasons: either because of the smallness of the rupture as mentioned, or because the intestine was inflated before by the entry of air.
So if this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by immersing a sponge or a piece of cloth wet in lukewarm water alone or perhaps cooked with  reed, and saffron, and cumin, and use it to wipe the intestine until the swelling subsides.
And that drink with cumin in it may be better than water alone in dissolving the swelling; and if you see the swelling has subsided, wipe the intestine with water cooked with  rosemary or bread-maker’s spice because it makes its entry easier and with less effort.
If his return is difficult after this treatment, make a small incision in the tear with the instrument used to open the sutures and the first treatment if successful is better than the incision. He is only forced to make an incision after necessity and the failure of other methods, and this is a picture of the instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, the other side is unlimited, and the thin end is not as sharp as a scalpel but is slightly blunt. It is an instrument that resembles a small plum, as you see. If the wound widens and the intestines enter, then it is necessary to return it to its natural shape.
And its special place, if you can achieve that, it is better. But if the tear is wide and in the lower abdomen, it is necessary to have the patient lie on his back and make his legs higher than his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, then his head and chest are raised higher than his lower body.
And also if the tear is on one of the sides of the abdomen, make your intention and purpose always towards the side where the surgery is higher than the other side. And thus it is necessary to use it in major surgeries and in medium surgeries.
But in minor surgeries, place it according to what you are capable of, then prepare a reliable assistant beside you who holds the entire tear with his hands and gathers its edges, then reveals them little by little for the person doing the stitching.
And this is the description of the common stitch, which is that you take a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound, then leave from the edge of the tear the thickness of the finger, and insert one needle without putting thread in the edges of the skin. With the edge of the peritoneum under the skin from the inside until it passes through it from that side, and the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum have been gathered, becoming four strands, then pull with a doubled thread around the needle from both sides several times until the edges of the wound meet tightly.
Then leave the thickness of the finger also and insert another needle and then intertwine it with the thread as you did with the first needle, so do not cease doing this until you have filled the entire wound with needles. Let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh and thick ones are difficult to enter into the skin, therefore they should be medium between thinness and thickness, and the needle should not be inserted into the edge of the skin too close.
So that the flesh is not quickly severed and the wound opens before it heals, nor should it be far from the stitch so that the wound does not prevent healing. Then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient when he sleeps and make for him pads from torn linen on each side to hold the ends of the needles.
And leave them until you know that the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with needles is thus suitable for small surgeries because it may be sufficient to stitch it with one or two needles or so.
Or as for the description of stitching for the second type, it is to gather with the stitch the four edges, meaning the edge of the skin and the edge of the peritoneum in one go with a needle containing a thread that is moderately thin and thick, then when it runs out of these four edges, return the needle from the side where it began itself.
So that the thread lies interwoven from above the wound to be the stitch according to the stitching of sacks, by which they tighten their cargo, and make between every stitch and stitch after the thickness of a small finger, and this stitch is known by everyone.
And with this stitch, I sutured a surgery that was performed on a man in his belly who had been wounded by a knife and the wound was more than an inch wide, and about two inches of the middle intestine had emerged from it, and the tear was in the middle of the abdomen.
After leaving the dressing outside the wound for twenty-four hours, the wound healed in about fifteen days and he was treated until he recovered.  He lived for many years afterwards, conducting himself well in all circumstances, and the physicians had ruled that he would never recover.
It is remarkable that I did not treat him with an ointment because I was in a place where there was nothing of the medicines, so I put on the wound cotton wool twice a day and used to promise to wash it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the kinds of special suturing, I mention it according to Galen's words: that you begin the suturing from the skin and insert the needle from the outside to the inside. And if the needle has reached the skin and muscle going straight along the length of the abdomen,
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle and inserted the needle in its other edge from the inside to the outside in the other edge from the outside, so when it has reached it, then I insert it again into this same edge from the outside to the inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and insert the needle in its other edge from the inside to the outside, and insert it with your insertion into the peritoneum to the edge of the outside which is on its side until it reaches all of it.
Then begin also from this side itself and line it with the edge of the peritoneum on the other side and bring out the needle from the skin close to it, then return it into that same skin and line the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge of the outside.
And bring it out from the skin on its side, and do that once again and do it again and again until you sew all the wound according to this example, and the whole description of this suturing is to sew a fur-sewing for the fur.
That the peritoneum be sutured once from one side with the edge of the skin and left once from the other side until it is emptied, as for the second type of special stitch which he also mentioned Galen this is his word verbatim.
And it is that he sutures it on the example of how some healers suture it, by gathering each part to its counterpart by necessity, attaching the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the edge of the membrane to its other edge, and this is as I describe to you.
It is necessary that the needle be inserted into the edge of the membrane close to you from outside and penetrate it inside alone, leaving the edges of the peritoneum, then withdraw the needle and insert it from outside to inside in both edges of the peritoneum, then withdraw it again.
And insert it from inside to outside in the other edge of the membrane on the opposite side, and this kind of stitch is better than the common simple stitch which was mentioned before, this is Galen's word verbatim, and some have said that a suture of the abdomen can be straightened with two needles. And that is that one thread be put into both of them, and you begin by inserting the needle from your side and penetrate it to the other side, and insert the other needle from the opposite side so that it penetrates from your side according to the suture of the scaffolding.
Know that if the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its suture is more difficult than other places in the abdomen. As for the treatment of the wound, its ruling in treating it is the same as that of all wounds, which is that if you find the wound fresh with its blood before air changes it and you have returned the intestines to their place and lined them up and secured them, then put salt on top of it.
If the wound has been changed by air, then put some of the ointments that you prepare on it until it weeps and the threads fall off and the peritoneum and membrane heal together, then treat it like the treatment of all wounds until it heals.
If medicines are not available to you, apply upon him from the beginning of the pus a clean cotton and change it twice a day as I have taught you until he is healed, for then you will not need other treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple,  if you fear that the wound will share pain with the main organs.
It is advisable to soak soft wool in lukewarm oil or rose grease and place it around the areas between the armpit and rib, for if he feels pain or a putrid smell in his bowels, this often occurs.  Then give him a slightly warm black astringent syrup.
And especially if the putrid smell has reached the bowels and become a wound piercing into his belly, know that what was from the bowels thick is easier to heal and what was from them thin is harder to heal.
As for the intestine known as the fasting one, it does not accept healing from a wound that occurs within it because of its abundance of vessels and their thickness, its softness, and proximity to the nature of the nerve. As for if what has emerged from the wound is pus and you have grasped it fresh, turn it back according to your hand's ability, whether on the intestine.
If it has passed for a while and has turned green or black, then it is advisable to tie it with a thread above the place where it has blackened so that it does not expose bleeding, for within the pus there are veins and arteries, then cut what is below the band.
And make the two ends of the thread hanging from the bottom of the wound outside so that it is easy for you to pull it and remove it when the pus falls and the wound weeps, I have mentioned the wound that occurs in the intestine, but if a tear occurs in the intestine and it is small.
It can be closed in some people because I saw a person who had been stabbed in his abdomen with a spear, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach. I stitched the wound, and it became a passageway from which feces and gas would exit. I treated him as if I did not hope for his healing. I continued to treat him until he was healed and the place had closed. When I saw that the place had closed, I feared for the patient that something bad might happen in his abdomen. However, nothing bad happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. Indeed, he eats and drinks and has intercourse and goes to the bath and dresses himself, and some experienced people have mentioned that when a wound in the intestines is presented and it is small, it should be sutured in this manner.
This is done by taking large ants, their heads then the edges of the wound are brought together, and an ant with its mouth open is placed on the edges of the wound. If it grasps and tightens its jaws, its head is cut off, so it adheres and does not separate, then another ant is placed near the first one.
And this is continued with several ants according to the size of the wound, then it is covered and the wound is sutured.  The heads remain attached in the intestines until they heal and there is no harm to the patient at all, and the intestines can also be sutured with fine thread.
Which is taken from the two sticks of the animal that adheres to it after it enters a needle,  it is taken by taking the end of this thread from the two sticks, and it is pulled well, then a thin linen thread is tied to its end, then that thread is put into the needle. And in it, the thread of the two sticks is used to suture the intestines, then it is returned to the abdomen, and this type of sewing with ants and sticks is only by way of hope and desire.
As for if the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy or cure for it at all.
Sixth and Eighty Chapter.
In the Treatment of Colds and Catarrh.
Know that every wound or swelling if it has aged and progressed and become an ulcer and did not heal, and it keeps discharging pus continuously, it is called in general in any member a fistula, and we call it a zecama, and the fistula.
It is on the truth a complex, intertwined, white solid, with no pain accompanying it, having a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some called it a feather. It is at times moist, constantly discharging pus, and perhaps the moisture stops in some instances.  And this moisture may be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And I have presented in the classification when mentioning surgeries that every ulcer that does not heal and flesh does not grow in it, this is due to one of nine reasons, one of them is either due to a deficiency of blood in the body, or due to its poor quality, or because inside it and on its edges there is solid flesh that prevents the growth of good flesh. Or because it is full of impurities and filth, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter that discharges from it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable in treating it, or because of corruption that has occurred in the city due to a plague, or because of something specific to the city.
Or because it is full of impurities and filth, or because the ulcer itself is foul and the matter that discharges from it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not suitable in treating it, or because of corruption that has occurred in the city due to a plague, or because of something specific to the city. As it was presented in the city of Saragossa where diseases are difficult to mature and ulcers take a long time to heal by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones within it,  and all these causes and their signs and treatments with medicines have been explained in the classification.
And here it is necessary for me to inform you about the treatment of fistulas and zecamas that occur by hand, know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body, from them are fistulas that end at large bones or veins or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum or intestine or bladder or rib from the ribs or vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or joint from the joints that are complexly assembled like the hand and foot, and some lead to near a main organ and so on.
If any of these fistulas are of this description, they are from the difficult diseases to cure that do not accept treatment and working on them is hard work and ignorance. If it were among the fistulas that did not reach these places mentioned and I hoped for and desired to treat them.
And I wanted you to reach that knowledge, so take a probe from copper or iron if the fistula passes on a straight path, then search for it with it. If there is elevation in the fistula, then search for it with a probe of thin lead because lead softens its body.
It may turn towards the elevation when entering, and if the fistula is with many mouths and you cannot determine them with the probe, then inject one of its mouths with a liquid, because the moisture that injects it will flow toward the other mouths.
Then inquire and examine in every way you can to know if there is a bone or nerve, or was the fistula's bottom far away or near, or was it one fistula with many mouths. Stop at all of this with the extent of your ability from interrogating the sick person and his condition of pain when you press on the place.
And such are the signs and causes that happen to the tumor, so if you have stopped at all of this with certainty, then proceed to treatment with confidence and examine if
If the abscess appears close at hand or in a healthy place far from a joint or nerve or artery or vein or one of the places I have told you about, then make an incision into the abscess as described above and remove what is in it of pus and rotten flesh and excess flesh.
And whatever grows in its mouth of flesh or tumor and the like, treat it until it heals. And if the abscess is deep and straight, it is necessary to make an incision into it as deeply as you can, then cleanse it of all its rotten flesh.
Then use the moistened gauze in the sharp medicines and insert it into the bottom of the abscess which you reach with the iron, do this several times until that sharp medicine eats up all that remains in the bottom of the abscess of decay.
Then close it with the ointments that grow healthy flesh until it heals. And if it does not heal by that, repeat what has been described above. If the cause of the abscess is bone and you are certain of this, then dissect it and make an incision into it as I have told you, unless something prevents you.
Sweat or nerve or main organ as we have said, if the bone is revealed to you and there is some decay and blackness in it, separate it until its decay goes away then treat it with what heals it until it heals. And if it does not heal and the pus continues as before.
Know that you have not reached all the removal of the decay, so reveal all that is on it again and thoroughly separate and cleanse it with all your effort then close it. If it heals, otherwise know that that decay is in a deep abyss from the decay, so reveal all that is on it again.
And thoroughly separate and cleanse it with all your effort, if the decayed bone was a small bone and you were able to pull it out, then pull it out with the fine forceps that are suitable for this.
If there are many bones, extract them all and do not leave any.  Make an effort. If something of the ligaments obstructs you beneath them and its cutting poses no danger, cut those ligaments and flesh if there is any and free the bones.
If it does not come to you to extract the bones from your time, put on the wound what covers it and leave it for days until what surrounds those bones of flesh and ligaments rots.  Keep the wound so that the incision which you made during your work does not close up.
But place on it if you fear that, cotton soaked in powdered sulfur with oil or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments; for if these rot the ligaments and the bones become separated, it will be easy to extract them and draw them out.
If a single large bone like the femur or tibia was involved and only its surface had been corrupted, strip it with a clean stripping until that blackness and corruption go away; then bind up the wound. If a large part of it had been corrupted and the corruption reached the marrow of the bone, there is no choice but to expose it.
And cut it all the way to where the corruption ends; then treat it until it closes up.  And I tell you of a case of suppuration that occurred to a man in his leg to make it an example and aid for your treatment, this man was young.
Around thirty years old, he was afflicted with pain in his leg from a cause that moved within his body until the matter reached his leg and there was a great swelling and abscess, which had no external cause; then time passed with the mistake of physicians until the abscess opened.
And much matter flowed from him and he was treated poorly until his leg ulcerated and there were many openings, all of which released pus and bodily fluids.  They treated it for a group of physicians for about two years; none of them were skilled in the craft of surgery.
Until they brought him to me I saw his leg and the materials were flowing from those openings in a great stream and the man had become pale, his body limp, so I inserted the probe into one of those openings and it reached the bone, then I examined all the openings.
And I found that some of them led into each other from all sides of the leg, so I cut and split on one of those openings until I exposed some of the bone and found it rotten, having been eaten away and blackened, putrefied and perforated until the hole reached the brain, so I disclosed what was revealed to me.
And I removed the rotten bone, and I think that there was nothing in the bone but that rot which I cut and disclosed, and that I had extirpated it. Then I made the wound close with cohesive medicines for a longer time, but it did not close, then I returned and uncovered the bone again above the first disclosure.
And I found the rot connected to the bone, so I disclosed what appeared to me also of that rot, then I threw away its closure and it did not close or heal. Then I uncovered it again and I kept on cutting the bone piece by piece until I healed it, but it did not close until I cut from the bone about a span.
And I extracted it with its brain, then I closed it with medicines and it healed quickly and was cured. But this repetition in his work and splitting was due to the condition of weakness of the patient and little possibility of healing and my fear for him to die because he was always experiencing from excessive diarrhea a foul discharge. And he recovered perfectly.
And flesh grew solid in the place of the bone, and his condition was settled in his body and his strength returned and he moved about in his affairs and nothing happened to him while walking that would harm him at all. If a bone has broken in a place in the body.
It is necessary that it be applied in this manner: Take a ligature and tighten it at the end of the protruding bone, and order him who stretches it to lift it up, and make another ligature of wool thicker than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone, and extend its end so that the flesh is pulled downward while you expose the flesh at the place you want to cut, lest the saw harm the flesh. And place a plank or board under the bone from below, firmly.
Because if you do this, the saw will not prevent itself from cutting the rotten bone, and it is necessary that the incision be above the place of decay a little so that there may not be in the cavity of the bone decay which does not appear on its surface to the touch, so you would be compelled to cut it again.
If the bone is rotten and does not protrude but some parts of it are connected to each other and the decay is in its center or in some of it, then expose the flesh from all sides of it, then place the plank from below, and then cut it from the first side where the decay is.
Then if you have finished cutting it, cut it from the other side and let the incision be a little distance from the decay as we have said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and cut the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the joint of two bones, there is no remedy except amputation. If the decay is in the metacarpals or metatarsals, the matter in its treatment is very difficult, but it is necessary to peel away the decay as much as you see and remove it and clean it as much as you can.
And by what means can it be straightened for you when a vein or nerve does not obstruct you, and know that the cutting tools and saws for cutting these bones are many according to the position of the bones, their shape, thickness, softness, size, smallness, hardness and splintering. Therefore, it is necessary that for every type of work, an instrument be prepared suitable for that work.
Know that the works themselves may guide you to the type of instrument you need if you have a long practice and knowledge of the arts of this craft, because whoever possesses the skill of craftsmanship and has witnessed various types of ailments. ...may deduce for himself what corresponds to instruments for every disease, and I have illustrated for you in the end of this chapter several instruments.
