The Seventh and Eighty Chapter.
In cutting off limbs and straightening bones.
A limb may rot, either from an external cause or an internal one; and if you have treated that corruption with medicinal treatment and it has not been effective, and you see the corruption spreading in the member, nothing can stop it.
Then you should cut that member where the corruption has reached so that the patient may be saved from death or a great affliction, greater than losing the limb. And the sign of someone who is afflicted with this is that the member becomes black until you think fire has burned it.
Or it rots after the blackness until that rot spreads to what is beyond that member and takes hold of the rest of the body, so hasten to cut it off. And also if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of some creatures like a sea scorpion or a snake or a viper and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay for the corruption to spread to the hand; and also if it has taken hold of the hand, cut it off from the base of the wrist and do not delay for the corruption to spread until it takes hold of the forearm.
And also if it has taken hold of the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself; if the corruption spreads and you see it taking hold towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for in that lies the death of the patient, and use other treatments to the best of your ability.
And do likewise to the man if gangrene has taken hold of the finger, cut off the finger at one of the joints. And if it has taken hold in the foot, cut off the foot in its entirety.  If it ascends to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the gangrene has reached above the knee, there is no remedy except to leave him and resign the sick person to death. The prescription for cutting off a limb or spreading it open is to tie a bandage below the place you want to cut, and tie another bandage above the place.
And one servant extends the lower bandage and another servant extends the upper bandage, and you strip away the flesh between the bandages with a broad scalpel until all the flesh is exposed, then it is cut or spread open, and it is necessary to place linen cloth from all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the healthy tissue, exposing the patient to added pain and a hot swelling. If bleeding occurs during your work, immediately cover the site or place some blood-stopping thorns on it, then return to your treatment until you are finished.
Then tie the injured limb with a bandage that suits its healing and treatment until it heals. And I tell you an example of a case of a man in his leg, this very condition which I describe to you,  that is, there was blackness on his leg with burning like fire.
And this gangrene was first to happen in his finger until it took hold of the entire leg. So he cut off the leg from himself when he saw the gangrene spreading through the limb with the intensity of pain and burning, cutting at the joint and healed.
And when a long time passed, that same gangrene appeared to him in his index finger. He came to me and I treated the limb with remedies after cleaning his body, but the gangrene did not recede and continued to spread in the other finger.
Until corruption took hold in his hand, and he summoned me to amputate it. I refused him out of hope for a favor from him and also feared for him if his hand was cut off because the strength of the man was on the verge of collapse. When he despaired of me, he turned back to his own country.
News reached me that he amputated his whole hand and was cured, but I have recounted this story so that it may be a help for what happens of the same kind of illness and to be a guide by which one can rely on and work with.
The Eighth Eighth Chapter.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicine.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and the duration of the swelling lasts until it gathers for a long time, then bursts or splits open and all that was in it of the pus comes out and the place remains empty like a vessel.
And the skin on it, like a cloth, has become slack and has not gone so far in its corruption that it affected bone or nerve or tendon. And from here it is worthy that it be called an abscess and it is not called an ulcer except if its duration lengthens until the corruption affects something of these organs.
Then it is called an ulcer or a chancre, and its treatment is that the whole of that skin be cut as it goes, especially if it has become slack and like cloth, and you are certain that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and it has not reached that degree of corruption, and there was thickness in the flesh, treat it with injection, which is to look at the abscess if it is large and the pus that comes out of it is foul-smelling, then inject it with the Egyptian medicine.
Which is honey, oil, and beeswax, equal parts, gathered in a bowl and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens to the consistency of honey, then take from it what you need and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess, tightly hold its opening, and leave the medicine inside for about two hours.
Then you remove it in the afternoon, doing this for days until the hidden place is cleansed and the stench disappears. And if you don't have this medicine, you may inject it with water of ashes, which is to take the ash of oak wood or chestnut wood and pour water over it.
Then you rinse it and inject it until you are certain that the hidden place has been rinsed.  If there is no possibility of giving the Egyptian medicine or water of ashes to the sick person, inject him with water and honey mixed with a little ground ginger.
Or inject him with honey and syrup mixed together because the honey cleanses and washes, and the syrup sticks to the hidden place, especially if the syrup has a lot of dried up residue. If you do this several times and are certain that the hidden place is free from corruption, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth there.
Such as taking from the palm ointment and dissolving it with rose oil and astringent syrup, or injecting him with some of the other medicines and ointments whose qualities we have proven in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the hidden place is narrow and does not accommodate the syringe tube.
Then widen it with iron a little or place a plug made of cloth soaked in the Egyptian ointment or the squill ointment until it widens.  And if the mouth is also wide, gather its lips with stitching and leave enough to accommodate the syringe tube without excess.
And if the mouth from which the pus flows is raised high, then cut it in its lowest place so that the pus flows downwards because when pus collects in the depths of the hidden place it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the hidden place downwards as you want, then form it so that the member is positioned in a way that pus flows easily from it according to what seems appropriate to you and so that nothing of the pus is completely retained within it.
As for the ointments that are placed on the wound from the outside and how they should be applied, take one of the plasters suitable for what you want and spread it on a linen cloth, and let the cloth be the size of the whole wound. Then cut with scissors opposite the opening of the wound, a hole slightly wider than the opening of the wound. Then spread from the soft ointments on another cloth, the size of the hole, and place it on top, placing it at a distance from the opening of the wound so that the pus can easily exit from it, and do not remove the large cloth until after.
For several days, as for the one on the opening of the wound, it is the one that you remove at all times so that what has gathered in it of pus can exit and to know what is flowing from it of pus whether it is much or little, ripe or unripe.
And with this also, inspect the place of the wound itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or not, and whether there is a swelling or not. If you persist in treating it like this and see that the matter coming out of the wound is slight.
Know that the wound has closed or is close to closing. Then, solve it from tomorrow or on the third day. And when you have opened the wound, replace the cloth with another and renew the ointment. If pus of a thin consistency comes out of the wound after a long time, do not despair of its closure.
Because it is likely to close because some people slow the growth of flesh in their wounds, and others are the opposite. If pus that is not ripe comes out of the wound after many days, know that the wound has not closed. If the closure of the wound is delayed.
And if the matter is prolonged, increase in drying the medicines and let them be medicines that are wet in their consistency and dry in their strength like the palm ointment if it has been increased with an excess of the black cumin seed. And one of the most effective treatments for this is to take the palm ointment.
He mixes it with rose oil and sprinkles it with aged, moderate wine, then he covers it with a plaster of this mixture. He can also do this if the palm ointment is not available to him when he has cooked the honey until thick and used it, or take from the myrtle, frankincense, and myrrh, grind them all together and sprinkle them on the honey while it is on the fire. Then he applies it on a piece of cloth and binds it to the wound or he coats it with honey that has been cooked until it thickens, sprinkles the medicinal herbs on top, places a piece of cloth over it and binds it.
And he may use in such case the long saffron, the root of white asphodel, and ground coriander, or chamomile used singly or collectively. Grind them all together, sift them and sprinkle them on the honey that has been described above and use it. If the wound remains for a long time and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption in its depth.
And he has treated bones or the rest of the solid nerves or ligaments with this treatment as mentioned before.
The Ninth and Eighty-Eighth Chapter.
In treating ingrown toenails, overgrowth of the nail and cutting off the extra finger, and separating fused fingers.
The ingrown toenail is a lot of flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or foot and perhaps in all fingers. If its duration is prolonged and treatment is neglected, it becomes swollen and hot.
Or it becomes rotten and festering until it eats the root of the nail and perhaps ruins the whole thing, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul odor and the tip of the finger becomes wide and its color becomes black. If you treat it with what was mentioned in the previous section and your treatment is ineffective.
Then it is necessary to cut all the excess flesh that grows from the nail with a knife, and then cauterize the wound afterward because cauterization in this case is very beneficial. As for if the bone was healthy and the nail was also healthy.
The corner of the nail, having been driven into the flesh and making it ulcerate and painful, one should place a thin piece of material under the corner of the nail that is driving into the flesh and raise it upwards, and cut that flesh gently.
And place on what remains of the flesh from the medications that burn and blister until it all disappears, then treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the decay has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone and remove it because it will not heal as long as there is decayed bone at all.
If you see pus and decay spreading in the finger, cut it at one of the joints as mentioned before, then treat it until it heals. If the nail is struck or wounded and there is severe pain, it is necessary to drain the pus first.
Then split the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique cut from top to bottom, and be careful not to reach the flesh under the nail because doing so will cause severe pain in the affected area and will be the cause for extra flesh to grow in the place, then treat the place with something that soothes the pain.
As for the extra finger that is born in some people's hands, it may be all flesh, and perhaps in some of them there is bone, and perhaps it has a nail, and its growth may be from the base of a joint of one of the fingers.
Or its growth may be in some of the joints of the finger, and those that grow in the joints of the fingers do not move, and those that grow near the joint of the finger may move, and what is made of flesh only, it is easy to cut it, by cutting it at its base with a wide scalpel.
But the one whose growth is at the base of the joint, its treatment is difficult, so you avoid cutting it, and as for the one that grows in the finger at one of the joints, it is necessary to first cut its flesh with a circular cut towards the bone, then spread the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat him until he is healed, and as for the adhesion that occurs to some fingers with each other, it often happens and may be something that is born with the human being and may be present when a wound or burn closes, etc., so it is necessary to cut that adhesion.
Until the fingers return to their natural shape, then place between them a piece of felt or gauze soaked in rose oil so that it does not adhere quickly and separate them, or put between them a thin sheet of lead until the place heals as it should.
And also if adhesion occurs to some fingers in the palm, cut that adhesion according to what is suitable and repairs the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On Fractures.
Fractures are twisted veins filled with black mucus that occur in most parts of the body and most frequently in the legs, especially in the tibia, fibula, and those who carry heavy loads, so it is necessary first to use a body wipe from black lead repeatedly, with a strong wipe.
Then expose the vein's owner, and as for treating it with iron, it is in two strokes, one of which is to cut it and remove the black blood, and the other is to draw the sweat and remove it entirely. As for cutting it, it should be done on this condition first by bathing the leg with hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then tighten the leg of the patient from above his hip to below his knee with a turban, and then cut the sweat in one place or two places or three wide cuts.
Then draw the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg to the top and from the top to the bottom until you remove the amount of blood that you deem sufficient or what the patient's strength can bear, then tie it up and order him to avoid foods that produce black lead, and repeat the vomiting and bleeding.
When the veins become full and this harms the patient, as for his leg it is on this condition that the leg of the patient if he has much hair is shaved then he enters the bath and stretches his leg in hot water until it reddens and sweats or does strenuous exercise.
If he does not have a bath to warm the limb, then the skin is cut in front of the vein a lengthwise incision either at its end at the knee or lower at the heel, then the skin is opened with forceps and the vein is peeled from every side until it becomes palpable.
And when it appears, you see it reddish and swollen. And when it is freed from the skin, you also see it like a tendon. Then, a thread is inserted under it until it rises and comes out of the skin, then it is tied with a blind, smooth forceps. Then, another incision is made near that incision the size of three fingers.
Then, the skin is peeled from over the vein until it appears, then it is raised with the thread as you did before and tied with another forceps as you did first, then another incision or many incisions are made if it is needed.
Then, he is pulled and cut at the end of the incision at the heel, then it is pulled and peeled until it comes out of the second incision, then it is pulled to the incision above it, and this is done until it is pulled from the third incision higher than all the incisions until when all of it has come out it is cut.
If it does not respond to pulling and peeling, then insert a needle with a strong, doubled thread and tie it and pull it. Then, insert the thread under it and grasp it with your hand in every direction until it comes out and be careful that it does not break because if it breaks, then it will be very difficult for you to peel it.
And a sharp instrument is inserted into the patient from it, and if you have peeled all of it, then put on the place of the incisions cotton soaked in syrup and rose butter or oil and treat him until he heals. If the vein is tightened and has ridges with a twist to directions.
If it is not straight as we have said, it is necessary to cut along each of its ridges and places of curvature, then hang it with pins until you pull it completely, and beware when cutting it that you do not cut or wound the vein because it will be difficult for you to tie it up, so be careful.
Image of the probe used to cut the fold.
Image of the blind pin.
It does not have a point like other pins and is not sharp at the end so as not to wound the vein, and it is thick in curvature and smooth because if it were thin it would cut the vein with its sharpness, but it should have an excess thickness as we have said.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In tying the veins of the city.
This vein is formed in the legs in hot countries like Hejaz and Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely populated, fertile lands, and perhaps it is formed in other places on the body besides the legs, and its formation is from a wound that occurs under the skin.
As it happens inside living bodies and in the worms, a boil, a worm, that is formed between the skin and flesh, and a sign of the beginning of this vein happening is that there is severe inflammation in the leg, then the place bursts, then the vein begins to come out from the place of that burst.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, and if its end appears, then it is necessary to wrap it around a small piece of lead whose weight is from one dinar to two dinars, and tie it and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
For every time something comes out of it to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it, and if it becomes very long, cut some of it and wrap the rest, and do not cut it from its root before all of it comes out because if you cut it, it will shrink and go into the flesh and cause a boil and pus in the place and a bad sore.
Therefore, it is necessary to manage and draw it out little by little until it comes out from its end and nothing remains of it in the body. And it may come out of this sweat gland in some people what is five spans and ten.
And it has reached me that it came out of a man for twenty spans, if it is severed during your treatment of him, then insert a reed into its hole and plug it with a long cloth along the body until everything in it is drained, and try to treat the place with medicines for days.
Then treat him with the treatment of tumors, and this sweat gland may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, so that it causes many openings and it comes out from each opening a branch, treating it as we mentioned in the division and what has come before.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
In the incision on the worm that is generated under the thigh and it is called the disease of the cow.
This disease is called in some countries among us the disease of the cow because it often affects cows; it is a small worm that is generated between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, ascending and descending.
It is felt by touch when it crawls from organ to organ until it ruptures where it has ruptured in the skin, and it comes out and forms itself from the putridity of some of the humors as it happens with worms and maggots and the gourd love in the belly.
And what is expected from its harm is that if it crawls in the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, perhaps it opens it and comes out, blinding the eye, and this happens often. If you want to treat it and remove it, then it will be when it crawls and appears to the touch.
Therefore, it is necessary to tighten what is above and below it with a strong bandage, then cut on it and remove it. If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it, apply heat to the place until it burns her, and the greatest thing that is expected is its damage to the eye as we said.
If you see it has become in the head near the eye, tighten under it on the forehead tightly then cut upon it and remove it. And it is necessary that the patient be treated with purification of his body by medicines that soften the bad humors and abstain from foods that cause corruption.
Chapter thirty-nine.
On the incision for the ailment known as nafar.
This ailment that is called nafar in our land is a pain that appears in some organs then moves from organ to organ, and I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was summoned to a sick woman in some fields, and she uncovered her arm, and I felt a slight puffing in the armpit of the arm's tendon. After an hour, I saw that puffing wriggle towards the shoulder like a worm, rising to her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
As it moved from place to place, the pain disappeared from that place and became fixed in the shoulder. Then I sat for a while and it flowed through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm. Then she told me that it was circling her whole body as I had seen.
So I was amazed by the speed of its transition from organ to organ, and before this I had not seen this ailment with my own eyes like this, in this way, except that I saw a group who found the pain moving from organ to organ, but I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I did not estimate this except that it was because the woman was from the people of the desert, dry-bodied, with exposed veins, so that from here the feeling appeared to the touch that wind moving and it is necessary that it would not appear on this measure in those who are comfortable and whose bodies are moist and hidden veins.
So if you want to treat it and the owner feels the pain, if it appears before you as we mentioned, tighten above it and below it quickly and cut upon it until that trapped wind is removed and the place is open.
If you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by rubbing the body and things that expel air and spread it, such as  the seeds of the musk mallow and the seeds of al-shira and similar remedies,.
Chapter forty-nine.
On extracting arrows.
Arrows differ according to their types and according to the places they are located in the body. As for their difference according to types, some are large and some are small, some are hollow glass and some are solid glass, and some have three angles and some have four angles.
And some have tongues and some have shards. As for those that are according to the organs they are located in, they come in two types: either the organs are hollow chief organs like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. So when an arrow is lodged in one of these organs and the signs of death which I describe to you appear, then it is necessary to avoid extracting that arrow from it, for death usually befalls its owner. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not concealed deep in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And the signs of the brain if an arrow enters it, piercing the bone and injuring the membrane covering the brain, are severe headache, fever, and dizziness.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not concealed deep in the organ, then extract it and treat the wound.  And the signs of the brain if an arrow enters it, piercing the bone and injuring the membrane covering the brain, are severe headache, fever, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes, and redness of the tongue, and stiffness, and confusion of mind, and seizure, and perhaps blood comes from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech is cut off and voice disappears, and from the site of the wound a white moisture resembling porridge flows and  it runs like water from meat.
So if these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the sick and extracting the arrow if it has not already been extracted. As for the signs of the arrow if it is lodged in the heart, near the left breast, and it feels as if it has been implanted in something solid, not in something empty.
Perhaps the movement of the arrow resembles the pulse and blood flows from the wound black, followed by coldness in the limbs, cold sweat, and paleness. Know that death has surely come, and a sign of the arrow having wounded the lung is the exit of foamy blood from the wound.
And the vessels that follow the neck swell and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes heavily and requests to inhale cold air. If the arrow has reached the diaphragm in the chest, it is close to the ribs, and breathing is labored with severe pain.
And it chokes and all the organs of the chest move, and if the arrow has reached the liver, that is followed by severe pain and blood exits from the wound resembling the liver in its redness. If the arrow has reached the stomach, perhaps something undigested emerges from the wound.
And its condition is clear, and if the arrow has reached the abdomen and it swells, and something of feces, or stool, or a ruptured intestine emerges from the wound, there is no hope for healing or removing the arrow. If the arrow has reached the bladder and urine exits.
And something emerged from it and the pain intensified on the patient, know that he is doomed. As for the rest of the organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, lower back, clavicle, thigh, leg, and similar organs, most likely they are safe.
When the arrow did not hit an artery or nerve and was not poisoned, and I tell you some of what I have seen regarding these arrows so that you may infer from it your treatment, namely, an arrow had struck a man in his eye socket at the base of the nose.
So I removed it from him from the other side under the earlobe, and he was healed and nothing happened to his eye. I also removed another arrow from a Jew who had been struck in the fleshy part of his eyelid below the lower eyelid, and the arrow had retreated, and I did not reach except for its small tip that is attached to the wood.
One large arrow of the composite quivers was a square piece of iron, polished and without barbs, which was made by a Jew and nothing went amiss in its making, and another arrow was extracted from the throat of a Christian, and it was an Arabic arrow.
This one had barbs, and it split between the windpipe and gullet, and it was lodged in his throat until he was brought before me and I extracted it, so the Christian recovered and was healed.  And another arrow was extracted from a man who had been pierced in his belly, and we estimated that he would die from it.
But when he stayed for about thirty days or so, and nothing changed in his condition, the arrow split and its shaft was moved, and I extracted it and he recovered and nothing went amiss with him. And I saw a man who had been pierced by an arrow in his back, but the wound closed over it.
But when it was seven years later, the arrow emerged at the root of his hip. And I saw a woman who had been pierced in her belly by an arrow, and the wound closed over it and the arrow remained, and nothing changed in her condition, and she did not find harm from it in any of her natural actions.
And I saw another man who had been pierced by an arrow in his face, and the wound closed over it and he did not find much pain from it, and there are many like this. And I extracted an arrow from a man of the Sultan's cavalry who had been pierced in the middle of his nose, which had tilted slightly to the right side.
And the arrow was completely gone, so I was called to treat him after the arrow had been lodged for three days, and I found the wound from the arrow to be very narrow, so I examined it with a fine probe and did not feel it, and he felt numbness and pain under his ear from the right incision.
So I thought that the numbness was from the tip of the arrow, so I covered the place with a poultice which had strength to draw and ripen, hoping that the place would swell and show a sign of the arrow, so I opened it up, and nothing happened in the place that indicated that the arrow had reached there.
And I continued to apply the dressing to it for many days, and no incident occurred. Then the wound closed within that period, and the patient remained in need of being supported from removing it for a period until he felt the arrow one day inside his nose, and he informed me of this news, so I placed on the wound the sharp medicine for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, and I felt the thin end of the arrow that stuck in the wood, then I increased in opening the wound with that sharp medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared to us clearly, and a period of time passed with me, about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and it became possible for me to enter the probes into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond to coming out, so I continued to soothe it and maneuver it with various tools until one day I grasped it with well-fitting probes, as its image appears in the end of the chapter.
And the physicians used to rule that the cartilage of his nose did not heal, so I healed it and the wound united, and the patient recovered completely with no harm at all. And I tell you how to extract some arrows so that this will be a measure and evidence for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and their details are not grasped by description nor encompassed by a book, but rather the skillful craftsman measures the little against the much, and what is present about what is absent, and he derives a new work and a new tool when facing strange occurrences.
If I descend from this craft, I say that arrows are extracted from the members in which they were lodged in two ways: either by pulling them from the place where they entered, or from the opposite side, and those that come out from where they entered.
Either that the arrow is prominent in a place of flesh, so it is pulled and extracted. And if it does not respond to coming out from its time when it entered, then it becomes necessary for you to leave it for days until the flesh around it decays, making it easier to pull and extract it. Similarly, if it lodges in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it also for some days and return to pulling and moving it every day; if it comes out, but if after several days it still does not come out, then it is necessary that you pierce around the arrow in the very bone from each side with a fine opening until it widens for the arrow, then pull it and remove it.
If the arrow is lodged in the skull bone and has penetrated one of the cavities of the brain, and some of those symptoms that I mentioned to you have appeared from the patient, then hold back from pulling the arrow and leave it until his condition clears up after several days.
Then attempt to pull the arrow and remove it, if it is deeply lodged and does not come out with pulling, then use the openings around the arrow as I described to you and treat the area until it heals, but if the arrow has gone deep into a part of the body. and is out of feeling, then search for it with a probe; if you feel it, pull it with some tools that are suitable for pulling it; and if you cannot do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow in the depths and there was no bone or nerve or vein.
Then make an incision until the wound widens and you can get to the arrow until you remove it, if it has ears, grasp them and free the flesh attached to them from each side with all the skill you can; hold back if you cannot free the flesh in the ear break and open them until you are freed.
And if you attempt to remove the arrow in any place, use your hand wrapped in cloth bands to all sides until you free it and be very careful so that the arrow does not break and make it difficult for you to pull it out; if you cannot do so in a timely manner.
Leave him for days until the flesh around it decays, then he will be easier. If bleeding troubles you, use what we mentioned in its chapter, and be careful not to cut any vein or nerve or tendon, and use your skill.
In every way you can free the arrow, and let it be with gentleness and care, and stabilize as described to you. And it is fitting for you that when drawing the arrow, you make the wounded person in the shape he was in at the time of the arrow striking, this is more suitable.
If you cannot do that, use what you can of the shapes. As for the arrow which comes out from the opposite side, either something may have protruded from it outwards, or you will find the tip of the arrow by touch from above the skin close to it and see it protruding.
So cut on it, and let the incision be to the extent that it can accommodate the fingers, then draw it out as it will be easy for it to come out. If it gets stuck in a bone, put your hand on rotation until the arrow affects the bone and widens itself for itself, then draw it.
Otherwise, leave him for days until you return to him until it comes out. If the shaft of the arrow is in him, push it with it. If the shaft has fallen and you want to use pushing, insert the jointed instrument into it so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it.
If the arrow is hollow, push it with an instrument that enters its cavity, as the arrow will easily exit by this means. If the arrow is poisoned, it is necessary that you cut out all the flesh which has become poisoned if you can do so, then treat it with what suits it.
If the arrow is in the chest or abdomen or bladder or flank and it was near what can be felt with a probe, and you could make an incision on it, make the incision and be careful not to cut any vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs suturing, then treat it until it heals. Image of the arrowheads that are used to attract arrows:
Their ends are like the beak of a bird, made as if it were a cold piece when it grips an arrow or anything else, and it does not let go. And they can be made in large, small, and medium types, all according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, the width of the wound, and its narrowness.

Image of the solid cannon:
This is solid at the end like a bolt to make it easy to enter into the hollow arrow and push it in,.
The Fifty-Ninth Chapter.
In  venting veins.
The veins that are customary to vent in the body are thirty, six of them in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the "sensitive" ones, and the two arteries in the visible temples, and the two veins in the hollows of the eyes known as the "lookers.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip from the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip, and these veins known as "jeharek," and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are vented in the arm and hand, there are five: one is the cephalic vein, which is on the outside (posterior) side and it is commonly called the "head vein," and the achilles tendon vein, which is the middle vein and it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch from the cephalic vein and it is commonly called the "body vein.
And the basilica vein, which is located on the inside (anterior) side and it is also called the "armpit vein" and it is commonly called the "belly vein," and the brachial vein, which is located on the forearm and it is the one that attaches to it, and the one that appears above the thumb clearly, and the ulnar.
This is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger, it has two branches, and in the leg there are three veins; one of them is beneath the kneecap from the outside side, the second is the saphenous vein and its location is at the heel from the inside side, and the vein of the groin and its location is at the heel from the outside side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them, as for the two veins behind the ears their benefit is that they be cut to treat chronic colds and migraines and psoriasis and chronic head sores of bad character, and how to cut them on what I describe.
And this is how; that you shave the head of the sick person and rub his backside in the location of the two veins with a rough cloth rubbed well then strangle the neck of the sick person with a turban until the two veins appear and their location behind the ears in the low places of the head so you feel them with your finger and where you felt their pulse under your finger there make a mark.
Then you take a sharp scalpel knife which is known as the neshil and then insert it under the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone then lift your hand with the vein and the skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin a clean cut and the length of the cut will be the length of two closed fingers.
Or similarly and you send from the blood the amount that you want then tie them up with sutures and leave them until they heal, and I have mentioned before in the beginning of the book how to cut them and cauterize them, as for the two arteries that are in the temples their benefit is that they be cut to treat chronic migraines, difficult headaches, constant blepharitis and a sharp flow of phlegm pouring into the eyes.
And how to cut them as I describe to you; the sick person tightens his neck with a turban until the veins appear for the touch clearly and their pulse is apparent under your finger, then mark it with ink and then raise the skin from above the vein upwards with your index finger.
You insert the knife to cut into the vein from below and raise it upwards, severing it as you did with the other veins. You then stem the blood flow as much as needed and then close the patient's wound and place your finger on the artery for an hour.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it, pulling it tightly from above, and leave it until it heals. I previously mentioned and described the [cotton] and [bandage], as well as their application, at the beginning of the book. As for lancing the temporal vein, its benefit is after lancing the cephalic vein for chronic ailments of the face such as scabies, ulcers, and redness.
And as for how to lance it, as I describe to you, the patient's neck is strangled with a turban until the vein appears. Then you take the instrument called the 'axe', and this is its image:.
You place the pointed spike at the head of the axe on the same vein and strike it from above with a comb or something similar to it, and you leave the blood to flow as much as you desire. Then, you close the patient's wound and tighten it until it heals.
And it may be opened with a wide scalpel, except that the scalpel should not be sharp-pointed like other scalpels, but rather slightly blunt-tipped, and you dissect it by turning because the bone is close, so perhaps the scalpel would break if it were thin.
As for the two veins in the hollows of the eyes, their benefit is for ailments of the eye from scabies, redness, and swelling, and diseases of the face. And as for how to lance them, it is that you tighten the patient's neck with a turban, then dissect them while you are standing over his head, and let the lancing be on a slight turn towards length with a small scalpel slightly wide.
Because the location has no flesh in it, and if the scalpel is thin-tipped, it might break. Then you stem the blood flow as much as needed and place cotton on them and tighten them for one night, then heal them. As for lancing the nasal vein, its benefit is from acute fevers.
And severe headache and diseases of the face such as the red running nose, especially if it is chronic, and how to treat it: The sick person tightens his neck and then you hold his nose with your left hand, and take a thin long scalpel.
And you insert it in the middle of the nostril itself between the two sides of the nose on a straight line because the sweat does not appear to feel there, so blood comes out from its point, and it is necessary that you concentrate your hand with the scalpel slightly and send out the blood as much as you need then tie it at night because it heals quickly.
As for the inguinal glands, their benefit is to treat shortness of breath and the beginning of leprosy and dark diseases that appear on the surface of the skin such as black leucorrhea, herpes, bad ulcers, and boils, and how to treat them: The sick person tightens them under his neck with a bandage and the craftsman stands on top of the patient's head.
And the sick person is sitting on a chair, then you cut the sweat to its length in a wide cut slightly, and you remove from the blood the moderate amount or according to what you see of the need for that, then do the same with the other sweat, then tie the bandage.
And you tighten the two sweats moderately so that the sick person does not choke and leave him until tomorrow because the wound heals, as for the veins of the jaw, their benefit is to cut them after cutting the jugular vein because it benefits from ulcers in the mouth, gum rot, bad ulcers, cracked lips, and bad ulcers.
Which are in the nose and around it, and how to treat them: Make the sick person sit in front of you and tighten his neck with a turban, then turn his lips and look at the two veins that you see one to the right of the lip and the other to its left, and distinguish them by their blackness.
And also that around them are very fine black veins, so cut them with a clean cut. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, aim to cut the larger and more obvious one, and do the same in the two veins in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is to cut them after cutting the qayfal for the vesicles that are in the throat from the disease of leucaemia and diseases of the mouth. And how to cut them is to make the patient sit between your hands with the sun's midday heat and raise his tongue and look under the tongue on one side a vein and on the other side a vein, their color is black.
So cut them and be careful not to overcut them because under them are arteries, and perhaps a bleeding of blood from those arteries might appear. As for the three veins that are cut in the elbow, they are the ones that have become customary to cut in all people, and their cutting is done in two ways.

As for the basilic vein which is one of these three veins, its benefit is to cut it because it draws blood from ailments that are below the throat and neck up to the chest and abdomen. Then you tie the arm and dissect the artery with a curved incision using a scalpel as we said, taking care that the blow falls away from the site of the artery. And when you see, upon tightening the ligature, swelling in the place you marked with lead pencil, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery so avoid it.
If you see the blood when bleeding spurt as urine spurts from a boy and the blood is thin red, know that it is from the artery, so immediately place your finger on it for a long time then remove your finger, if the blood stops, often it will stop, so tighten the arm.
Leave it, and warn the patient from neglecting it, let him be on his back and do not move him for days until he heals. For the place where the wound is small and the ligature can be placed on the arm, inform him. But as for the jugular vein, the benefit of dissecting it is that it draws blood from above the head and below the body because it is composed of a branch of the carotid artery and a branch of the cephalic vein, as we said.
It is necessary for a surgeon to be careful when making an incision, because if there is a nerve beneath it and he increases the depth of his cut and reaches the nerve, numbness will occur which makes it difficult to see, and perhaps it will not heal at all. This nerve often appears sensitive to touch.
If it is hidden in some people and is fine and not apparent, it is necessary to make your incision over it a slit with the probe and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, cut the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of cutting it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for diseases of the eyes and it is necessary in this vein, especially if you want to cut it with a  knife, either the olive knife or the broad wind knife because it is the safest of all veins since there is no artery or nerve beneath it.
Except that when you cut, you must avoid the head of the muscle with the knife and aim for the soft place, and it does not harm if it is not hit by the first blow that it be recut several times. However, perhaps it will swell in some people if it is not cut on the first blow.
But that swelling does not harm it at all, and as for the method of incision and its effects and what should be done to repair it, first of all you must know that incision is either used only to preserve health.
And if it is used for diseases, and the one used to preserve health and its continuity and protect against the occurrence of diseases, then the incision should be in one of the three veins in the arm, namely the cephalic, the basilic, or the axillary, and it should be at the beginning of spring when signs of fullness appear.
And it should be cut on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed in the day, and as for the incision used for diseases, it has no limited time but whenever need and urgency call for it, from night or day.
At every hour and in every time, it is not fitting to bleed boys until they reach fourteen years of age, and the elderly who have passed sixty years, and if someone resolves to bloodletting for any reason whatsoever.

And one should also beware of bloodletting after a fever, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, wakefulness, fasting and all that depletes the strength, whether physical or mental, then look at the thinning of the humors before that if the blood is thick.
With foods and drinks and medicines if he is able to do so, then he enters the bath if nothing prevents him or practices some exercise so that the blood flows and makes his bloodletting in the middle of the day as we have said, and he strives to empty his chest of all bad psychological states that day.
Such as worries, anger, fear and from all bodily ailments such as excessive fatigue, exertion, intercourse, and the like, and prepare his gathering with things that have become customary for people to use from types of perfumes and incense and amusements and the like, each person according to what he is able.
Then he sits the bloodier on a pillow that is higher than the pillow on which the one being bled sits, then he brings out his arm and the bloodier rubs it with his hand twice or three times then he tightens the bandage with the tourniquet and winds it twice or three times, let the tightening be moderate.
For when the tightening is not moderate, either by excess in tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is towards relaxation, it also prevents the flow of blood, then after the tightening it is fitting that he rubs the one being bled's hands all together with some pressure on each other.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the surgeon wipes the area with a little of old oil, especially, then he places his index finger of the left hand on the same vein under the place he wants to cut slightly so that the vein does not retreat and avoid the blow. Because some of the veins you find like tendons, they retreat when cut, and some are filled with air, so when you place the scalpel on them, they sink under the scalpel and deceive the surgeon, and the scalpel does not open the vein. If it opens it, then its opening is narrow.
Therefore, it is necessary that the surgeon be steadfast and take his time in all of these things, then he lowers the scalpel. If the vein opens from its first pass, otherwise, he will aid it another time under that place a little or above it quickly if the place does not swell.
If the place swells or the patient feels pain, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage because it may bring hot phlegm and he does not enter the bath, then he will repeat the bleeding if he wishes. If the scalpel entered and the opening was small and the blood flowed thin.
And I feared that the blood would not come out in the amount you want, so return the scalpel to the same hole gently on a straight line and increase the opening a little and do that quickly before the place swells because many people may swell at the small opening.
If you see that it has swollen, do not add to it at all because it does not help you. Place on it something of heated oil because it eases the flow of blood and it is better in this place than the oil itself and all other ointments as well.
So use heated oil in all your bleeding of the veins when the flow of blood is difficult, and this remedy can do it, the elixir al-Faruq and shakhznaiya if you place one of them on the place because the blood will run and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large tumor occurs in the place of phlebotomy, it often happens, especially to those who have never been bled before or whose artery opening was small, then immediately place a sponge soaked in warm water and salt on the tumor and hold it for an hour, as it will dissolve.
It should be done after the blood has come out of the artery itself completely or from another artery, if anything remains in the place after days, something black or green, it does not harm, but if you wish, apply something of saffron and bitter-dissolved to it.
Or something of the juice of fennel and the like, and often a tumor and swelling occur when bleeding from the  bassalik so place your hand on it, if you find it hard at your touch, then that is a bad swelling, be careful not to put anything of what was mentioned on it.
For it may bleed from it an artery's blood but clot it with what is in it, so that it tightens the place then treat it with all treatment until it heals, and it is necessary to withdraw for every person from the blood according to their strength and what appears of the color predominant on the blood.
For if the blood is black, let it come out until it turns red, and so too if you see it thick, send it until it becomes thin, and so too if it is sharp until its sharpness goes away, and it is necessary for those who are full of strength and need to withdraw the blood in one go.
That he broadens the artery's phlebotomy and the lancet is wide, and for those who are weak, the opposite of that, and it is necessary to withdraw it in several times and the hole is narrow, and the best thing used in phlebotomy is that it is a twisted and boiled cut not a prick.
And this type of phlebotomy is safe from bleeding and cutting a nerve, and it is better and more secure than phlebotomy by length and width, and for those who are accustomed to use the cupping at the time of phlebotomy, it is necessary to feed them before the phlebotomy something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or quince.
If he has been lanced and the blood has been drawn from him three or four times, and if his temperament is cold, let him take before phlebotomy bread soaked in syrup of amber or syrup of honey flavored with licorice, or in good, fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during phlebotomy and the cause is excessive blood loss, it is necessary to give him broth water and thin fragrant syrup, and use a compress of galangal on his chest with rubbing and apply all that has been mentioned in the section on fainting.
That which results from vomiting, but if someone wants to relieve his arm and move his blood again, it is necessary for one who had a phlebotomy with much vomiting and weak strength that the blood be moved little by little according to strength over successive days.
But if someone wants to relieve his arm and move his blood again and his body was strong, let him do that after seven or nine hours from his first phlebotomy, but if someone wants to draw blood from his body to the opposite side to which it leaned, it is necessary for him to be bled on the second or third day.
But if someone has a lot of blood in his body, which has become hot and caused fever, it is necessary that blood be drawn from him in one go, drawing out much quantity and widening the incision until he faints after all the conditions of phlebotomy have been met. And place your hand on his pulse while blood is flowing so that death may not occur instead of fainting, for this often happens if the phlebotomist is ignorant and forgets, and it is not necessary if you want to relieve the arm and move the blood again.
And if the armpit opening has closed and blood flow is difficult, do not scratch it hard or twist with force, for this is very bad; either leave it until he is bled again or use a knife to scrape away what has clotted from the armpit opening.
Or place on it something of salt dissolved in water, or place on it something of the Faruq elixir or the shaknazina and poultice it with a fine poultice until the blood comes out, if the sweat gland has swelled, leave it.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides, if necessity demands to drain blood again, then either dissect it above that place or dissect it in the other arm or in the other sweat gland.
As for dissecting the arm's nerve, it is dissected instead of the akhal and basliq if they are not found or are hidden because it is composed of them, and the way to dissect it is that the patient puts his hand in hot water until the joint reddens and the sweat gland appears clearly, then bind above it with a bandage slightly moderately tight.
Then dissect the sweat gland with a slight twist, neither sideways nor lengthwise, let the opening be wide and your dissection of it above the wrist joint be slightly, if blood is difficult to come out, then return the hand in the bowl of hot water and let the blood flow in the water.
Until it reaches your need, if you are in the summer months, you may dispense with returning the hand to hot water and most of what makes blood flow in hot water is in winter, and dissecting this sweat gland is safer than all the veins. Because there is no beating sweat gland or nerve underneath it, as for dissecting the asilim from the right hand, it is beneficial for diseases of the liver and the way to dissect it is that you bind the wrist of the hand with a bandage or with your hand after putting it in hot water until the sweat gland swells. And it becomes very clear to the touch then dissect it with a slight twist, even if you amputate it entirely, it does not harm him at all, be careful not to overdo your hand with the scalpel because there is a nerve under the fingers and the place is bare of flesh, then return the hand to hot water.
And leave it to flow blood within it, for if you do not return it to hot water the blood will clot in the mouth of the vein and cease to flow. Then when you have extracted from it as much blood as needed place on the vein grease and salt so that it does not adhere quickly.
And similarly it is necessary to do this with every narrow branch, and as for the benefit of its separation from the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen and you do this in its separation just as you did in the second [example], and as for the incision of the saphenous vein, its benefit is for illnesses in the lower body such as ailments of the uterus.
And retention of menstruation and illnesses of the kidneys and chronic sores of the thighs and legs and suchlike diseases, and how to separate it: he puts his leg in hot water and rubs upon it until the veins become filled then ties above the joint of the leg with a bandage and the vein its place at the heel towards the thumb.
And it branches out in the face of the leg into many branches so separate it in its widest branch or at the heel where they meet, this is better and safer. If you separate it in the face of the leg then take care from the nerves that are beneath on the face of the leg.

Then let him repeat the incision slightly higher up, for the place is safe and there is no fear of harm from it if you guard against the nerve as we said, and do this with the saphenous vein of the other leg similarly. And the benefit of its separation is for pain in the hip if that is from hot blood, and how to separate it: he puts the sick person in the bath and hastens and ties his leg from below the hip to above the heel with four fingers using a thin long bandage, for it does not appear otherwise.
If it appears, then dissect it in any case, either by twisting (which is better) or by cutting off a part of it or making a cut across it.  Since its location is healthy and in most people very subtle, if you don’t find it...

Then loosen the tightness and place a piece of cotton on the site of the incision, and tighten the area, since it quickly heals.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On cupping and how to use it.
Cupping vessels can be made of horns, wood, copper, and glass. Cupping is done in two ways: one with the condition of removing blood, and the other without a condition.
And this cupping without a condition is done in two ways.
And the cupping of the nape, which are both sides of the neck, and cupping of the chin, which is below the lower jaw from the mouth, and cupping of the shoulders, and cupping of the spine on the tailbone, and cupping of the elbow pits, which are in the middle of the arms, and cupping of the calves, and cupping of the ankles.
Cupping draws blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason it does not expel force like lancing, nor should cupping be used by fire or without fire in any disease that is caused by congestion until the body expels everything.
If the need for cupping arises from disease or by custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end, in the middle, and in any other time.
And that is because some people, if their blood increases excessively to the point of needing it to be removed by cupping, find heaviness and headache in their heads; and some find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck; and some find itching in their face and side, darkness, and congestion in their eyes; and some itch where they have been cupped.
And some laugh excessively, and some find the taste of blood in their mouths, with a burning sensation in their gums and spitting out blood; and some sleep more than usual, and some see in their dreams blood, redness, carnage, and wounds and the like. So when we see any of this...
Especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed from midday.  As for the benefit of cupping the nape of the neck, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that pours into the eyes.
But it is necessary that this be done after completely emptying the body, and this cupping may be a substitute for opening the cephalic vein. It should be avoided by those who are cold-headed or have a head cold, as it will harm them greatly.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly or those with cold-headed diseases, and excessive use of it has caused forgetfulness in some people.
Therefore, it is necessary to order the cupper to lower his hand with the cup slightly downward for fear of inducing forgetfulness; as for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for opening the acupressure points and the basilica vein.
Therefore, it benefits from asthma, shortness of breath, tightness in the chest, coughing, and fullness. It is necessary to raise cupping the shoulder slightly because if it is lowered, it causes weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the occiput, it is beneficial for aches that occur in the head, cataract, migraine, angina, and pain at the roots of the teeth, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy. It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to deepen his hand with the instrument lest he cut a blood vessel and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum rot and similar diseases of the mouth, and it can take the place of phlebotomy of the vessels in the lips. As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations that result from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the flanks, it is beneficial for what benefits phlebotomy of the three vessels: the basliq, akhal, and qifal, because it draws blood from all those fine vessels in the flesh and draws those fine vessels from other thicker vessels until the drawing reaches the three thick vessels.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper not to press too deeply because the place is covered with little flesh and under it are nerves and blood vessels. As for the single cupping that cups over the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids of the anus and sores of the lower part.
It is necessary to instruct the cupper that the cup should be large and made of copper because the place needs a strong suction and perhaps a glass cup might break, and it requires many precautions. As for cupping the legs, it reduces fullness noticeably.
Because it draws blood from the whole body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder, and it brings on menstruation and is beneficial for boils and carbuncles, and it takes the place of phlebotomy of the safijnen and ‘arqoqeen vessels, except that it weakens the body a lot and causes dizziness in most people. Cupping the ‘arqoqeen has benefits similar to cupping the legs.
The manner of placing the cautery is that the cautery is first placed empty and suctioned moderately, but do not prolong the placement of the cautery, but rather place it quickly and remove it quickly so that the humors may enter the place in an even influx, and continue to repeat this until you see the place has become reddened and opened.
And when redness of blood appears, then it is bandaged and suctioned slowly again, and you look at the condition of the bodies. And if anyone's flesh was loose and porous in the pores, it is necessary that it be bandaged once only so that the place may not be crushed.
And order the surgeon to widen the bandage and deepen it a little, and adjust the suction gently with rocking and gentle movement. If there is blood that is thick, then it is necessary that he bandaged twice. As for the first time, let him open a way for the clear blood and its wateriness, but as for the second time, it is to investigate the expulsion of the thick blood.
And if the blood was very dark, he repeats the bandage a third time to reach the end.  In general, if we want to expel a little blood, we suffice with one bandage, but if we want to expel a lot of blood, we bandaged more, and if we estimate that the blood is thick.
Then it is necessary to bandage deeply, and the moderate limit in the bandage is only the depth of the skin, what is not necessary to use from ointments when placing the cautery and from waters and what the surgeon warns against. As for those whose skin was thick, hard, rough, and with narrow pores.
Then it is necessary to lubricate the places of the cautery with opening, softening, and dissolving ointments, but if it was in summer time, such as the ointment of khariji or the violet ointment or almond butter or pumpkin seed butter.
But if it was in winter, such as the daffodil ointment or the lily ointment or the chamomile ointment or the hyacinth and the like. If the phlegm was thick and cold, then let the ointment be marjoram butter or camellia butter or banana butter or spikenard butter and the like.
If the cupping is wide in its suction and flesh is sucked, it is necessary to abstain from oil, and these people should wash their cupped areas after cupping with rose water or cold water or grape juice water or squash water or men's water and the like. But as for those whose blood is much moist.
They should wash their cupped areas with vinegar or water of the acacia and sumac and the like. But as for those whose phlegm is thick, they should wash their cupped areas with old syrup or myrtle water or the broth of the shrub or chamomile and the like. And it is necessary to beware of cupping in the bath or after the bath.
Rather, it is necessary to use it after leaving the bath for an hour or two and no one should sleep after cupping. What should not be done by the cupped and the treated before and after cupping.
It is necessary to look first, if the cupped or treated one is jaundiced and the dominant on his blood is the edge and inflammation, it is necessary for him to take cold things like pomegranates, chicory with vinegar, lettuce, senna, and ginger and the like, and make his food the squab pigeons and mutton scrambles and rump steaks and the like.
And if his temperament is cold, it is necessary for him to be watered with honey syrup and metha syrup or Senna buzurri and he should consume the fragrant wine that is between the old and the new, and he is commanded to eat little food and make his food squab pigeons, partridges, sparrows and dove chicks scrambles. And it is necessary for the drink on the day of cupping and bleeding to be more than food.
And it may be necessary for some people to be watered with the Faruq elixir or musk medicine or chilth before cupping and bleeding or after it to strengthen the main organs and thin the blood, and it is not necessary to water those who are heated.
As for the dressings that are without a condition, they are those placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, and location of the kidneys and hip because these organs do not tolerate being conditioned, but rather it is intended to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing a dressing on the breasts in the case of hemorrhoids or we use it to resolve cold air that has stagnated in the organ, such as placing a dressing on the abdomen and navel, for it loosens the organ, warms it, and removes pain to resolve that air.
And it may be placed on the kidneys if a blockage or stone occurs in them, and by the strength of drawing it, perhaps it will open the blockage or remove the stone from its place, and it also does this if placed on the liver and spleen when there is air that is tangled within them. These dressings may be used empty with only the application.
And they may be used with heat and may be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of colic, that is to say, fill the dressing and make it large with hot water alone or with water that has been boiled with some herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed filled on the location and held and removed and repeated as needed, and this is the image of the dressing that is used with heat:
Its mouth opening is two fingers wide as we have depicted, and its depth is half a cubit, and there is a small hole on the side of it, about the size that a needle can enter, made from Chinese copper or yellow copper with a thick border.
Flat and smooth to not harm the organ when placed upon it, and there is a crossbar of metal in its center made from copper or iron where the candle is placed with heat, and this dressing may be made large, larger than what we have described, and small according to the illnesses.
And as for its use, the dressings of boys and thin people are not the same as those of men and strong bodies, and as for how this dressing is placed with heat on the organ, it is that a wick with heat from tightly woven cotton or a small candle made from wax is placed on the center of the erected penis which is in the center of the dressing.
To make the fire ascend upwards towards the lower part of the cautery so that it does not burn the patient’s body, then it is placed on the member and the finger on the hole mentioned until when we hold the cautery as needed, the finger is removed.
And the steam emerges through that hole and the cautery melts at its place, then the wick is advanced as described and returned if it is needed for that, but as for the cautery used in the treatment of scabies with water, there is no rigid rod or hole in it.
And it is only used by filling it with water and placing it on the member only, and this is its image: .
And this cautery, the larger it is to hold a lot of water, the better,.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On treating a tumor.
The cautery is used in most cases in the organs that cannot be placed with a cautery either because of their small size like the lip and gums, etc., or because the member is exposed of flesh like the finger and nose, etc..
And how to use it, you should intend from the cautery that is in fresh clean water, free of impurities, then leave it for a day and a night in fresh water until it dries and there is nothing left in its belly. Then the body is bled first by cupping or bloodletting, then the diseased member is wiped.
Until it reddens, then it is placed on it and if it fills it falls off, and if possible the place is touched by the cautery, it is more effective in benefit, otherwise wash the place with vinegar then with a lot of water and massage and squeeze it, if it persisted bleeding after the cautery fell and that was a discharge, then wet a linen cloth in cold water.
And place it on top until the discharge stops, if the blood increased sprinkle on it turmeric powder or  a similar substance from the astringents until the blood stops or half a peeled onion is placed on the place.
Leave it until the paste adheres in its place, for the bleeding will cease, and if it becomes necessary to reattach it, do not reattach that particular patch if another is available. If the patch refuses to adhere, then wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the area until it brings out something of the blood, then apply it, for if she feels something of the blood adhering to the place, it will stick. If you want to remove it, scatter some plaster, salt, or ash on top of it, and it will fall off its place.
Chapter Three.
On Bone Setting.
This chapter also belongs to the knowledge needed in the practice of medicine, namely setting bones and treating fractures that occur in the bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by the ignorant among physicians and the common folk, and by anyone who has never skimmed a book on this subject from antiquity or read even a single letter from it.
For this reason, this art has become neglected in our land, and I have not encountered anyone who has mastered it even a little. And I have only benefited from it as much as I have because of my long readings of the books of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them until I extracted this knowledge from them, then I adhered to practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have presented to you in this chapter all that encompasses my knowledge of the subject. And my experience has been applied to it after I have brought it closer to you and simplified it, shortening it to the utmost extent, explaining it with utmost clarity, and depicting many images of the instruments used in it for you.
As is customary in the previous chapters, for increase clarity, and there is no power except by God, the Most High, the Great.
Chapter One.
There are beautiful and comprehensive sections on the matter of bone fractures, and it must be presented and before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary in the beginning of this chapter to mention summaries and chapters. You must first try to understand them and grasp their reality, you and anyone who is eager to learn this noble craft other than yourselves.
So I say that when a fracture, or dislocation, or sprain or fall occurs to anyone, it is necessary first to hasten to its division or loosening or both. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength or if the one affected by this is a child or an old man or if the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be restricted to legumes that are cold and the meat of birds and small game, and he should abstain from drink, heavy meats, and overeating of any food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling subsides and there is no expectation of fluid seeping into the site.
Then, the patient should return to his first usual regimen. But if the fractured bone is in the process of setting, then the patient should eat foods that are rich and heavy, thick and containing fat like lentils, rice, wheat berries, barley, and beef marrow. And eggs, fatty fish, and thick drink, because with this regimen the consolidation of the fracture is faster and better. Know that fractured bones in men who are heavily built and elderly never fully heal to their original nature.
For their bones are dry and hard, and although what was fractured in the bone may be very soft, like the bones of young children, nature will sprout on the broken bone from all sides something resembling glue, thick and sticky, which holds it together and tightens it until it binds one part to the other.
He binds some parts to others until it comes in the utmost strength and solidity as it was at first, so that nothing hinders its actions.
For this reason, it is necessary that the food of the patient be made of foods that are strong and cohesive and thick as we have said, and know that a fracture may differ in its kinds according to the difference of the limbs because the fracture of the bone of the leg is different from the fracture of the bone of the head and the fracture of the bone of the chest is different from the fracture of the bone of the back.
And so all the rest of the limbs are different from each other, and we will come to mention each type of fracture explained in its chapter detailed separately from the others, and a type of bone fracture may also differ within itself.
Because it may be a fracture that is broken without there being fragments, and it may be a fracture along the length of the bone and the fracture is fragmented with protrusions that are separated and not separated, and the fracture is with a wound and rupture in the skin, and the fracture is a slight crack.
And for each type there is a special way of healing it as will be mentioned in detail in its place, and of what distinguishes a fracture of the bone its deviation and unevenness and its appearance to the touch and its crepitus when you touch it with your hand.
So when there is no visible deviation or crepitus or feeling of disturbance when you touch the bone, and the patient does not feel much pain, then there is no fracture but it could be a sprain or a slight fracture or a slight crack so it is not necessary to move it with stretching and pressure at all.
But carry on it the medicines which will be mentioned after a while that suit the place, and then tie it with gentle pressure, and know that if the bone is broken and shattered into two without there being fragments, except that each part has moved from its place.
It is necessary for you to hasten from that time to set it right and straighten it before a hot swelling occurs in it; if a hot swelling occurs, leave it for some days until the hot swelling subsides, then straighten it in any way you are able by gentleness and skill.
And know that setting and straightening it is easier than a bone that has splinters in it, then it will be tightened on what will be mentioned later; if the bone has splinters, there is no escape from extending the broken limb from both sides, whether hand or foot.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with a rope and hand; let your placement of the limb be on an even surface according to its natural shape until when the broken bone part extends, then at that time return those protrusions to their places in every way you are able by skill and gentleness.
And strive with all your effort that you do not cause pain or suffering to the patient; strive that you bring together one bone with its mate in the best form, and it is necessary at that time to feel them and sense them with your hand; if you see anything amiss there, correct and straighten it to the best of your ability.
And beware of excessive stretching and forceful pulling as many ignorant people do, and they often boast of doing so with a hot swelling or inflammation in the limb as you may have seen from their actions repeatedly.
Then after setting, perfecting and tightening that limb, impose stillness and rest on it, and warn the patient not to move it during his waking hours or sleeping, or when changing position, restless, or when sneezing, and all movements to the greatest extent of his ability, and let him be careful to make the position of the limb a position that he feels safe from pain.
And that whenever he feels pain or discomfort in his position, the limb should be moved to a different position that does not cause him pain, and let him be careful that this position is level and straight.
Lest deformity occur in the limb if it is straightened, and as for how to pull together a broken limb, I will describe it to you; know that broken limbs differ in their smallness, largeness, and shapes; and what is small among them such as the arm, finger, and knee and similar things.
Bandages of linen should be thin and light for the limbs that are delicate, like the thigh, back, and chest; they should be thick bandages because the wide ligament needs to hold the large limb and pull it together from every side with even tension so there is no defect within it.
Then, after you have finished with the setting, start applying the appropriate plaster. On a flexible strip on the site of the fracture, then start winding the bandage over the same place of the fracture three or four times, according to what the limb deserves, and pull your hand slightly with the bandage, then take it to the upper side from the location of the fracture.
With a pull less than the pull you gave to the fractured location, then widen with the winding away from the fracture site slightly and relax the tension gradually until you take something good from the correct position, then take another bandage and wind it also on the fractured site.
Winding, then take the winding to the lower side of the fracture, let your action in pulling and relaxing the winding be as mentioned in the first upper winding, then put between the bandages a piece of flexible strip or linen that fits the deformity of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, otherwise, do not put anything in it, then wind another bandage on top of it and set the firm plaster on these bandages from your watch if there is no swelling or hot tumor in the limb.
If there is swelling or hot tumor, apply what soothes that tumor and removes the swelling, leave it for days, then tighten the plaster on it; let the plaster be from carefully carved half-reed strips or let the plaster be made of wood shavings.
Those that are made from pine or palm bark or willow or fir, and whatever else is available of this sort, and let the width of each splint be according to this pattern and shape exactly.
Except that the splint placed on the fracture itself should be a little thicker and wider than the other splints, but as for the length of the splint, it is made according to what suits the broken limb from its size, Image of the splint:.
