Then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening exactly, and then tie them from above with firm threads according to what we mentioned about the tightening, which is that your tightening should be more at the site of the fracture. And the closer it is to the fracture, the less the tightening.
And the threads should be of medium thickness and softness, and they should be from wet cotton especially because if the threads were thick as you see some ignorant people do, making their threads from unravelled cotton strips, which is a great mistake.
Because the tightening with them falls outside of moderation, and thin threads are also not suitable because you cannot achieve what you want with the tightening, and there should be no less than a finger's width between the splint and splint.
If the patient is hurt by the edges of the splints after tightening in the correct places, put under them from soft cloth or fluffy wool so that it does not hurt him from that at all, and if the fracture is with a wound and there is a tear in the skin, we will mention it separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be tightened with splints from the first day, because if the limb is large, it should not be placed on splints until after five days or seven or more according to your safety from the occurrence of the hot swelling.
And this is the description of the bandages that were used to bind them by the ancients who were placed on fractures and dislocations, a general bandage description that binds the fracture and is suitable for most temperaments, especially boys and women because it is not excessively hot nor cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the mill which is the heart of the fine flour which adheres to the walls of the mill when the millstone moves, and you knead it as it is without mixing it with egg white and make its dough neither thick nor thin and then you apply it.
The characteristic of another plaster that sets a fracture, dislocation, and sprain, is taken from wool, resin, oak gall, saffron, frankincense, and asafoetida, ten drachmas of each, and mastic and patience, five drachmas of each, and twenty drachmas of turmeric, and twenty drachmas of Armenian or Roman clay. Everyone is ground and mixed with turmeric water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then this plaster is used, because it is well-composed, sets broken bones quickly, and is suitable for most people because of its moderation.
The characteristic of another plaster that also benefits for a fracture and sprain, is taken frankincense, wool, and white mastic from each ten drachmas, mastic and patience, five drachmas of each, six drachmas of oak gall, twenty drachmas of Armenian clay, everyone is ground finely and sifted and kneaded with water or egg white and used.
A characteristic of a plaster for joints and bones that have moved from their places and soothes the pain that accompanies them and the cracking of the bone and fracture, is taken the boiled wool dipped in vinegar and cooked oil and placed on the location, this plaster does not have the strength of setting.
But it is good at soothing hot swelling and relieving pain especially, characteristic of another plaster that sets a broken bone, is taken the dried fig leaf and opium poppy leaf and they are all ground and plastered with them while wet, characteristic of another short plaster used when setting a broken bone.
And if you want to analyze the rest of the swelling, take from the root of mastic, chamomile, and violet and ground carnation from each a part, grind them all and knead it with paint if the member is not inflamed, if it is inflamed then knead it with watercress water or plain water and use it.
A poultice stronger than the first one is used when a hard tumor occurs due to a bone injury. It is taken from the root of the Al-khtama, cotton wool, fenugreek, King's Crown, Merzenjosh, violet and  Babungo, one part each.
They are pounded and kneaded with lukewarm water or fresh water or paint, all depending on the heat of the member and its stillness. As for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book to be placed on the broken limb when it heals.
Except for the cauterized materials made of wax and oil, and he described it to be moderate between thickness and softness. As for Galen, he saw that things containing caulks with something hot like frankincense, myrrh, mastic, and similar to what we mentioned, should be placed on the broken limb when healing.
A poultice that is beneficial for weakness and pain, it takes equal parts of barley, lentils, human hair or bird feathers, Al-khtama, and salt. It is pounded and sieved and kneaded with it, and as for the amount that should remain tied.
Then, if you look and if the patient does not experience pain, itching, or movement of the broken bone from its place, do not remove it for many days. If there is severe itching in the place or disturbing pain or swelling,
then act quickly and remove it immediately and do not delay that at all, and remove the poultice from it. Then take a soft cloth or sponge clean and wet it in lukewarm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain calms, then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then, place the padded cotton dipped in vinegar and oil or rose grease on it, and bind it to him for a night until the hot tumor is prevented, and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain goes away. Then return it to light tightening and the slight poultice and do not tighten it like your first time.
And treat it until it heals, and if you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and puffiness and all symptoms have disappeared altogether and it requires a bandage and tightening, then do as you did at first. And if nothing of what has been mentioned happens in the limb...
...then do not treat it except after three or four or five or seven days, and he may leave it for twenty days, all of that according to what appears to you from the state of the limb as we have said until when it becomes necessary for a fracture and the flesh is close to clotting.
Also, when that happens, add more tightening than your first tightening, and also increase the thickness of the patient's food as mentioned before. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried up and becomes loose more than necessary, know that the food is prevented from reaching it.
So wait with lukewarm water at every time you treat it, and let that be every three days, and slightly loosen the tightening. For by this action, food flows to the limb and it heals quickly, as for what the ignorant do, the ones who forcefully break the limb again...
...for if it doesn't force the first time properly and forces it crooked, then it is a mistake from them and a great error. And even if it were correct, they would have mentioned it in their books at first and practiced it. I haven't seen any trace of that from anyone among them. And the right thing is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On the Fracture Occurring in the Head
The types of fractures occurring in the head are many and its forms are different and its causes are varied. Some fracture is from a sword blow, and it may be that the bone breaks completely until it reaches the peritoneum under the bone... like how it happens in wood.
And for this reason, this type of fracture is called a "comminuted" fracture. Or it may be that the sword cuts some of the bone and breaks its surface only and does not penetrate to its end, and this type of fracture is called an "avulsion" fracture. And the wound of these two fractures may be either large or small.
Of fractures there are some that are comminuted or splintered, and this is caused by a blow from a stone or a fall on a stone or the like. This fracture may be penetrating, having nearly reached the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures may also be either large or small. And of fractures there are some that are hidden within the bone, in the delicate hair's breadth, which is a slight crack.
Therefore this type of fracture is called a hair-like fracture. And there are fractures that occur from a fall or a blow from a stone or the like, where the sheet of bone enters inside, and the place becomes dented, as happens to a piece of copper if it is struck by a blow, causing part of it to enter inside.
And this most often happens in the soft bones of the head, like the skulls of children. And all these types of fractures may have fragments that are clean or unclean, and I will mention their treatment all together in its place, and we shall recognize all these types of fractures by examining them.
And examining them with probes and removing the rotten flesh from them, and as for the hair-like type, it is known by revealing the bone and wiping it and rubbing over it with a stylus, because the fracture appears black. And as for the treatment of the fracture, first one looks to the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms what clearly indicates fear, such as vomiting bile and distention, loss of mind, cessation of voice, fainting, high fever, bulging eyes, redness of the eyes, and the like of these symptoms, do not approach the patient nor treat him, for death is imminent to him with these symptoms in most cases inevitably.
And if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and hope for his safety, then take care of his treatment, because if the injured person comes to you at the very beginning of the injury and this was during the winter months...
It is necessary that you strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day at all events, and if it is in summer days it is necessary to hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day so that what is under the bone of the membrane does not rot and expose those symptoms we have described.
If the bone fracture reached the membrane covering the brain and there was crushing and bruising, it is necessary to cut the crushed part as I describe to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in any way that is possible for you and according to the shape of the wound and what may be concealed from the patient, if when you uncover the bone, there is bleeding or a hot tumor, then correspond to it with what is appropriate.
Which is to stuff the place with a cloth soaked in syrup and rose oil and clean the wound until the tumor subsides and bleeding is stopped, then take in repairing the bone and removing it which is done in one of two ways of working.
As for the one way, it is to cut the bone with a delicate incision narrow blade and this is its picture:.
And then you use another incision after this incision wider from it slightly and this is its picture:.
Then you also use another cutting instrument, which is wider than the second one, and it should be obligatory for you to have several different cutting instruments, some being wider than others and some shorter than others, and their edges should be very sharp. They should be made of Indian steel or good steel.
And use the back of your hand to strike the cutting instrument so that the skull doesn't shake and get injured,  if the bone is strong and firm then it is necessary for you to make holes around it before using the cutting instruments with the holes they call non-occluded holes.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is behind it, because the hole has a rounded shape below its sharp head similar to a collar or small circle which prevents it from plunging and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take several of these holes, each one suitable for the thickness of that bone until it prepares for every skull a sharp pointed end of its type in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and this is the image of three types. Of the holes large, medium and small:
As for how to drill around the fractured bone, it is that you put the hole on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has pierced through, then move the hole to another place and make after each hole a space equal to the thickness of the lead or its equivalent.
Then you cut with the instruments between each two holes and do that to the best of your ability with gentleness as we have said until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the tools you have prepared for it.
Like the  guts and the small instruments of gentleness, and it is necessary for you to be very careful that the hole or the cutting instrument does not touch anything from the peritoneum, and if you have strengthened the bone and it has separated from the peritoneum if it was attached to it and brought it out then it is necessary for you to remove it.
Smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the cutter, except that it ought to be finer and gentler than all the other cutters; if anything of the small bones or splinters remain, take them carefully with what you have of instruments.
Then treat the wound afterward with the ointments and salves mentioned, as for the other side of this operation it is an easy side far from complications which Galen has mentioned and praised highly. This is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin by uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is most severe and well-known, so that when you have uncovered that part you make under it the point of this saw instrument. And here is its image:
The saw part of it should be smooth, not cutting anything, and the sharp part of it at its sides going in length as you see, so that the saw part should rest against the peritoneum and the face of the sharp part on the bone, then strike upon the instrument from one side.
With a small hammer until you cut all the bone gently as it revolves, and you are safe from the membrane that no frightening accident occurs to it, even if the operator is the most ignorant of people and the most cowardly, yes, even if he was asleep.
If anything from the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places of the bone, scrape it off with the saw part itself and get rid of it gently, for it gets rid of it without harm or fear.
As for if the fracture of the bone did not penetrate to the membrane and what was severed from the bone its face remained and there was roughness and splinters, gentleness, it is necessary that you remove that roughness and remove those splinters with a gentle tool, which has been made of several different sizes so that you can use one of them in each place most suitable and appropriate according to what guides you in the work.
The broken bone and its shape, and it is necessary that you use in your setting first the wider of the splints and then after that use a finer one from it and continue doing so until you reach using the finest and most delicate of them all.
But as for the rest of the small fissures and delicate fractures, it is necessary that you use in each one a treatment according to what leads to its improvement, which is something not hidden from him who has in this craft the least degree and stood on what we have written and explained in the large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, it is necessary that you take a linen cloth to the size of the wound and soak it in rose grease and place it on the mouth of the wound, then take another doubled or tripled cloth. And soak it in syrup and rose grease and place it on the first cloth, and do that with the lightest you can manage so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then apply a wide bandage above it and do not tighten it except for what holds the wound only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of the hot swelling, then cover it and after that use the medicines that come with it, such as dried lily roots, powdered  turmeric, finely ground coriander seeds, saffron and the like, which is to make from these medicines poultices.
And apply on the wound as they are dry, and in general, use a medicine that is suitable to clarify and does not burn, and take care with all your effort while treating the wound that it be clean, without the remnants of grease or dirt and do not leave pus to accumulate in it at all.
Because if pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and inflames it, causing great harm to the patient, and this may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If you look at the surface and see blackness, and the patient presents with the symptoms we have mentioned, know that he is inevitably going to die. If the blackness resulted from a medicine applied to it, and that medicine had the power to cause that blackness.
You should take from the honey a portion and from rose grease three portions, and beat them well together. Then smear a cloth with them and place it on the peritoneum, then treat it with the types of treatment necessary until he heals.
Chapter Three.
In setting a broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except for its upper part, both of them or one of them, because they are bone, as the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break, but rather it is subject to contusion, deformity, and dislocation.
So if one of the parts breaks, it is necessary to insert your little finger into the nostril and set that fracture from the inside with your index and thumb from the outside until you return the nose to its natural shape. Let this be done gently by you and take care that what you do does not cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and your finger cannot reach it, then it should be set with a blunt instrument that has a little thickness. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. Let him hasten to set it on the first day of the fracture.
If it is possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling has subsided, then insert a wick of cotton cloth into one nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the wicks be thick to the extent that they fill the nostril, and some of the early setters have mentioned that the wicks should be soaked in butter and changed every day. I do not see that, but rather it is necessary that the wicks be soaked in egg white mixed with flour dust.
Then, the wound is left until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens; and a tube of goose feathers may be inserted into the nostril after covering it with a thin layer of linen, so that the fracture of the nose is more firmly held in place, and lest the patient be prevented from breathing.
This is not something that is necessary, if you wish to make it and if you wish you can make the ligatures; however, if a hot swelling appears on the nose during your work, apply cold compresses to the nose with qayruti or cotton soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something from the Diachilon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, it is necessary to compress it from the outside with fine semolina and finely ground lentils mixed with egg white, then place a soft bandage on top and do not bind the nose to anything at all, for if the nasal bones are fractured in a slight fracture.
Or if it is shattered, then you must split it open and remove it with the instrument suitable for it, then suture the incision and treat it with ointments that promote healing and closing of wounds; if a wound occurs inside the nose, then it must be treated with sutures and use lead pipes. until it heals.
Chapter Four.
In setting fractured beards...
If the lower beard is fractured and not broken with a wound, if its fracture is from the outside only and does not break into two and protrude inward, then knowing it is easy.
And it is necessary that if the fracture is in the right side, you insert your index finger from the left hand into the mouth of the patient, and also if the fracture is on the left side, you insert your index finger from the right hand and gently raise the bump of the fracture from inside to outside.
And your other hand from the outside of the bone controls it to straighten, if the jaw fracture has broken into two, then it is necessary that support be used on both sides to align it until it can be straightened, and if there has been a tooth eruption.
Or if it is fractured, tighten the thread as much as possible, whether gold, silver, or silk, then place a strip of linen on the broken bone and place a folded bandage on top of the linen and put a large tight splint over the bandage or a piece of leather insole, equal in length to the fracture. Then tie it from above according to what seems appropriate to you, tying it in a way that matches its pressure so that it does not break, and instruct the patient to rest and stay still, and make their food soft meals. If you think something has changed in shape from any angle, attend to it on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to what seems appropriate to you, tying it in a way that matches its pressure so that it does not break, and instruct the patient to rest and stay still, and make their food soft meals. If you think something has changed in shape from any angle, attend to it on the third day. Then repair what it covers with flour of mill mixed with egg white or fine semolina after removing the splint from it, and put a soft cloth on top of the bandage. As long as that bandage sticks to it and the bone hasn't changed, leave it.
Do not remove it until it heals and the fracture strengthens, as this fracture often strengthens in three weeks. If a hot swelling appears during that time, use what we mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until the swelling disappears.
As for if the fracture is with a wound, if any bone shard or shards have healed from the bone, gently remove those shards using whatever tool you deem appropriate. If the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel to the extent you need.
Then, after removing all the shards, if it was wide, draw the mouth of the wound, and otherwise, apply one of the ointments suitable for it. And stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On Setting a Broken Collarbone.
The collarbone is most often broken from the front, at the end of the shoulder socket, and it breaks in one of three ways: either it breaks and splinters into two without creating any shards, which is the easiest to set.
As for a fracture that has splinters, it is more difficult to set. Or perhaps the fracture is accompanied by a wound, and in this case, if the fracture is without a wound, two assistants should be brought: one of them should hold the arm following the broken collarbone, and the other should extend the neck toward the other side.
Then you straighten the fracture with your fingers until it takes the proper shape and there is no projection or irregularity. If more extension is needed, then under the armpit of the afflicted one, a ball of cotton or wool should be placed.
And the bone should be as you need, and extend and raise the collarbone, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened properly. If you cannot pull the tip of the collarbone outwards because it has gone deep, then the afflicted one should lie on his back.
And under his knee, a medium pad is placed on the bone, and the assistant presses his knee down until the collarbone deep in the bone rises up. Then fix the fracture and straighten it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the collarbone has broken and is moving, then it is necessary to cut through it and gently remove that splinter. If the splinter is stuck in the bone, try to cut it with one of the pieces prepared for that purpose. After the area under the collarbone becomes the instrument that preserves the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron, and this is its image: .
It resembles a spoon without any irregularity, and its width is according to your need for the size of the bone. As for its length, it is also according to what you can work with, and let it have two ends as you see, one end wider and the other narrower.
If the wound you made when removing the splinter of bone is wide and the hot swelling has been contained, gather the lips of the wound with sutures. If the tear is slight or you fear the hot swelling, pack the wound with cotton and gauze according to the extent of the tear.
If a hot tumor appears, then apply a poultice of flour, vinegar, or wine to the opening of the rose hip.  As for a bone sprain if it is without a wound or cut, then apply a compress made from mill dust mixed with egg white onto the bone. And you place a flexible splint on top of it, and then place a ball under his armpit if he needs it. Then take a long turban that is about an inch wide.  Then apply a bent board on the splint and compress, then take a bandage from thin wood. Its width is three fingers, and in length as well. Then insert it into a cloth and insert that cloth with the bandage in the place of the turban that lies over the fracture site, then tighten the turban on the fracture as you would normally. And wrap it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under the armpit of the sick person, returning it repeatedly on each side, and as you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly, which is not hidden from you.
And the entire point of all this is that the bandage should not be removed from the fractured bone. Therefore, it is necessary to examine the patient every day. Whenever the bandage slackens and you see that the splint has shifted, then repair it and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on his back, and put a small cushion under his armpit while he sleeps at night.
So that his back is raised from his side and the clavicle fracture rises with the elevation of the shoulder or tie his arm to his neck. Do not loosen the bandage unless an incident occurs in the area, such as a boil or tumor, for twelve days.
Then renew the compress if you see a reason for it. Return in the tightening and leave it until the fracture of the clavicle heals and forms a callous, becoming stronger over twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people less than that.
The sixth chapter.
In setting a broken shoulder. It rarely breaks in the broad place of it but rather its edges, so when one breaks from its place or in the middle of it and this is known by touch, then according to what the fracture's shape is, set it...
Set it and return it to its natural shape in every way you can, then upon the place put a powder of flour with egg white and  flexible bandage and place on top a splint made from folded linen and then place upon it a broad splint made of wood.
Thin to the extent of the whole shoulder or slightly wider, if under the splint there is a wound in one of the places of the shoulder then set that wound with a flexible bandage until the splint lies flat, then tighten above it with a long scarf.
Tighten it firmly and make sure the splint does not move from its place, and inspect the bandage every day, and whenever it loosens tighten it and set the splint if it moves from its place, let the patient lie on his sound side, and the shoulder is set in twenty...
days or twenty-five days, and if this number is completed then remove the bandage and you are safe because it is from bones that do not fear breaking them or their splintering.  If a splinter of bone emerged and was embedded under the skin then make an incision upon it and remove it. And do what has been mentioned in a broken collarbone, from soothing the hot swelling if anything of that happens,.
The seventh chapter.
In setting a broken breastbone.
The breastbone may break in its middle, and it rarely occurs, but rather its edges are more likely to break and shatter. And from its signs if it breaks in the middle that it tilts downwards and causes him great pain and difficulty in breathing and coughing.
Perhaps blood is seen and the fracture in the bone becomes apparent and is not hidden from the sense, and when it is set he must lie on his back and a pillow is placed between his shoulders then his arms are pressed together and the ribs are brought together by the hands from both sides and care is taken to make it even on every side as much as possible.
And it is adjusted until the shape of the bone returns to what is proper, then a bandage and splint are placed on it and over that a slab of thin board from cypress or lutewood or the like in lightness after having wrapped it in a cloth. Then care is taken in tying it on the broken bone so that it does not move, and the bandage is passed around to the back several times and tightened tightly, then the bandage is examined at all times, and whenever it relaxes it is tightened again.
And if necessity demands that it be undone at any swelling or pain or tumor in the place, then hasten to undo it and remove the bandage and correct what has appeared of that with its treatment, then return the bandage if you see reason for that. And fasten it tightly until it heals.
The Eighth Chapter.
In setting the ribs when they are broken, know that the fracture occurs in them in the strong places following the back and their extremities from the front,  it is exposed to it because of its being cartilaginous and knowing this is not hidden from the sense when examining with the fingers.
And set it by making the fracture even with the fingers on the proper side until the shape is equal to what is proper, then bandage and tightly splint the broken bone if need be, and if the rib fracture is inclined inwards it exposes the sick person to severe pain.
And a fracture like that which is exposed to him who has pleurisy because the bone fractures the diaphragm and exposes him to shortness of breath and coughing and expelling blood a lot, this is difficult treatment, and the ancients have been ingenious in it with many contrivances.
Some say that the food of the sick person should be what produces wind and gas so that the abdomen swells, stretches, and the fracture is driven outward. We dislike this lest it become a confirmation of the occurrence of the hot tumor, if it has not occurred. If it has occurred, then it increases in it and confirms it.
And some say that a plaster should be placed on the site and then sucked with force, which is similar to the method except that he fears that the plaster will be drawn excessively to the site due to its weakness. And some say that it is necessary to cover the site with wool that has been dipped in hot oil.
And it becomes a pad between the ribs until it is full, and the bandage becomes level if it is wrapped on a turn, then the patient is treated with treatment of the choicest food and medicine, if the patient's condition is severe and he cannot bear it. And the bone would pierce the diaphragm in a painful manner and we feared for the patient, so it is necessary that we should cut at the site and expose the broken rib, then we put under it the instrument that preserves the peritoneum, which its description will be given, and we cut the bone gently and extract it.
Then we gather the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching and treat it with ointments until it heals, if a hot tumor appears during that time, then act quickly by placing a poultice in rose fat on the site and treat the patient with what calms the tumor from within also.
And he lies on his side that is easier for him to sleep on until it heals, .
Chapter Nine.
On setting a fracture of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they are fractured and rarely do they occur, and most of what happens to them is injury, as well as the vertebrae of the back also, if this occurs to one and you want to know whether it will heal or not. Look, if you see his hands stretched out and numb and dead and he is unable to move them or extend them or grasp them, and if you prick them or pierce them with a needle he does not feel it and finds no pain in them, know that he will not heal in most cases, for he is doomed.
And if he moves them and feels them when pricked or pierced, know that the bone marrow is intact and the patient will heal with treatment. If the vertebra was affected in the same way, and you want to know whether it will also heal,
Look at his legs, if you see that they are stretched out and the same thing happened to them as in the hands, then when he lies on his back, air and stool exit without his will, and when he lies on his stomach, urine exits without his will. And if he lies on his back and wants to urinate, he is unable to do so, know that he is doomed, and do not treat him. If none of this happened to him, the matter was lighter, and the treatment for what happened is to try to soothe the hot swelling.
By putting on the afflicted vertebra, rose oil alone or with roasted egg yolks, you should apply it three times a day until the hot swelling subsides. Then place one of the supportive absorbent bandages over the area and tie it with a bandage, and order him to remain still and quiet.
And he should not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain until he heals. If a fragment of bone or something else has occurred due to the injury and has healed, it is necessary that you cut open his skin and remove that bone. Then, stitch the edges of the wound if it is large, and then treat it with ointment until it heals. If the last bone of the sacrum, which is the coccyx, was broken, you should insert your left thumb into the anal opening.
The broken bone is smoothed with the other hand according to what is possible and achievable, then a bandage and splint are placed on it if it is needed, then it is tightened.
If you feel a broken piece in it, split it open and remove it, and treat the wound with the treatment mentioned above until it heals.
The Tenth Chapter.
On Setting a Broken Hip.
It rarely happens that the bones of the pelvis break, but if it does occur, the fracture is usually into fragments at its ends, a lengthwise split, and a turning inwards. This brings about pain in the place of the fracture to the patient, and paralysis and numbness in the leg on the side where the fracture occurs.
And its setting involves passing your hand over it until you feel the fracture, how it is shaped. If the break is only at the ends, then smooth out that fracture according to what seems suitable to you, so it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is lengthwise or if it has turned inwards, place the patient on his belly so that you can set that fracture. After smoothing it out, apply a bandage to it, then put a splint of wood or leather on it and tighten it firmly.
Do not fear that the fracture will shift or that the splint will come off, and smooth out any bump from the bandages to fill it until the tightening takes hold in a straight line. Tell the patient to sleep on his back or on his right side, if a hot swelling appears for him.
Then stop stretching it and setting it until the hot swelling subsides. Apply what soothes it as mentioned before, then return to setting and tightening it properly. If there are fragments or splinters in the bone, do not remove them.
Do not touch them but smooth them from the outside as we mentioned and leave it tightened until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is that which lies between the elbow and the shoulder; if it breaks, its setting is on one of two sides. One side is to take a stiff, smooth splint of medium thickness in this manner:
And tie it at its ends with two bandages, and suspend it from a high place and have the patient sit in a chair, then place his fractured arm on the splint until his armpit becomes attached to the center of the bend of the splint, and suspend something heavy above it.
Or extend it downwards and the physician straightens the fracture with his hands until the fracture returns to what is right, and the other side is that the patient lies on his back and suspend his hand from his neck with a bandage.
Then order two servants, one above the fracture with his hands and the other below, to extend each one in their direction, and if you want the extension to be stronger, tighten under the fracture with a bandage and above it with a bandage, and extend each of the servants in their direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder, it is necessary for the [middle] of the bandage to be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow, and similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, it is necessary for the bandage to be in that place and on the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture gently without force until the fracture is level and well aligned, then tighten it if there is no hot tumor, but if there is a hot tumor, leave tightening until the seventh day and place a poultice moistened with vinegar and rose oil on it.
Until the tumor subsides, then tighten it. The prescription for tightening is to carry the bandage on the fracture, then carry a new linen roll on the bandage, and gather the arm on the humerus itself and place his hand open on his elbow.
The torn and stretched limb is supported on the arm and shoulder so that the arm acts as a splint if nothing prevents you from doing this, and nothing changes with the fractured bone. If you fear something will change with it, then use splints.
It is that you put on the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than all other splints, and let there be between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an increase of three fingers from each side.
Then you tighten the splints with the tightening which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
It is that your tightening on the place of the fracture be the strongest.
And as much as the fracture is farther away, the tightening is less. If you see the placement of the splints and tightening as we have said at the time you set the member from its time, then do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, leave the tightening and splints for seven days as we have said, then inspect the bandage every three days lest there be a boil or puffing in the place, or the nourishment from reaching the member due to excessive tightening, so that you correct all of this as we mentioned.
If you are confident that nothing of that will happen, do not remove the bandage until after many days, and let the patient lie on his back with his hand on his stomach, and put under the shoulder a stuffed cushion from wool moderate.
And inspect at every time of night or day lest the shape of the fractured limb be broken or the bandage slacken, so that you correct all of this with your effort, and make the food of the patient according to the rank which we have presented, so that the food be gentle first until when the bone begins to harden.
Then it is necessary to thicken his food, for it is customary for the arm and leg to harden in forty days, so when then it is necessary to remove the bandage and use the bath, and treat with ointments that are good for it, if the fracture was open and suppurating.
Do not remove the bandage for fifty days or two months.
Chapter twelve.
In setting a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius, one small, lying next to the thumb, and the ulna, larger, located below the small bone, so perhaps the ulna has broken alone or the smaller one alone, and maybe both have broken together.
So when the small upper radius is broken, its setting is easy and healing faster, but if the lower ulna is broken, it's a bad break to heal with difficulty and slowness, and worse of all if both bones are broken together. If the bone that broke was the small upper radius.
then the doctor when setting it should make its extension gentle until it aligns, if the large ulna was broken then he must make its extension stronger and if both radius bones were broken then he must make the extension much stronger.
Then it should be placed with the hand shaped when setting and extending on a cushion, with the thumb higher than all the fingers and the pinky lower than the rest of the fingers, and the patient sitting upright on himself.
And let the cushion be equal in height to him so that the patient doesn't have difficulty.
Then an attendant extends the arm from below either with his hand or a bandage, and another attendant extends from above as well, then the physician sets the bone until he puts it back in the best shape possible, if there are shards of bone in the break they are to be placed together.
Put every shard back in its place to the best of your ability, if a shard appears protruding and it is tearing the skin, don't try to set it, instead cut into it and remove it as we mentioned previously. If the fracture is with a wound, I have made a separate section for that.
Take its treatment from there, and if you find a hot swelling in the first stitching of your fracture, mix an astringent with qyrot's, prepared with rose grease and white wax, let it be moderate between thickness and thinness and tighten the wrapping on it gently until the swelling subsides.
Remove the qyrot's, and put on the bandage prepared from almond meal mixed with egg white, then carry the splints, and let the splint placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger, and know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture is in one radius or in both, then make your tightening on the site of the fracture stronger and tighter, and every time you tighten towards the top or bottom, make the tightening slightly looser as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
Let the wrap that covers the fracture be a soft, moist one and not too hard, and let the thread used to tighten it be made of special cotton that is moderate between thinness and thickness as we described, and inspect the limb and its ligaments after some days.
If something happens that needs to be repaired, such as a sore that occurs in the limb, it is necessary to apply warm water to the limb until the sore subsides and leave the limb untied for a night until it rests, then tighten it again.
If the tightening has relaxed and the bone has moved or something similar, repair all of that with your best effort, and also look if the nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightening, then it is necessary to loosen it a little and leave it for days until nourishment reaches it, then tighten it.
If nothing of what we mentioned has happened to the patient, then he should not be moved until after twenty days or so, then hang the patient's hand around his neck and let his arm be moderate, and he should take care from agitated movements and let him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm is set in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps in twenty-eight days, all of that according to the conditions of temperaments and the conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
In Setting a Fracture of the Palm of the Hand and Fingers.
The knuckles of the palm and joints of the fingers rarely fracture but are often bruised. So, when a fracture or bruise occurs in the palm...
It is necessary for the patient to sit upright with a chair at his level before him.
Then he places his hand on it, extended, and the bone attendant stretches it out and the physician aligns it until, when it has been well set, then it is necessary to apply the bandage and splint, unless a hot swelling occurs, then a plaster cast should be applied from above according to the position.
And it has been recorded on a soft sheet, and if the fracture is towards the bottom of the palm, then make a shape of a sphere from cloth and order the patient to grasp it with his broken hand, then tighten with a long cloth.
Let the splint be from skin that is soft so it will touch the skin with the bending of all the palm and tighten it as much as necessary, if the fracture is outside then it is necessary to make the splint from above and another splint from below in the palm so that the hand remains open and erect.
Then the tightening is carried out as the hand turns and the fingers are intertwined with the bandage, if the fracture is across one of the joints of the fingers then if it is the thumb then let it be smoothed out as necessary then tighten it with the palm, and if you wish to make a small splint for him to erect the fracture.
And do not move it, if the fracture is for the rest of the fingers like the middle finger, index finger, ring finger or little finger then let it be smoothed out and bound with the following healthy finger or bind them all together on the allegiance, this is better or place a small standing splint on them.
As we said in the thumb and inspect during your setting of it and after it from the hot swelling, then confront it with what is necessary whenever something of that happens as has been repeated its description.
Chapter Fourteen.
In setting a fracture of the femur.
The femur often breaks and is felt because it turns forward and backward, and its setting is done by tightening a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture and the patient lying on his face.
Then each bandage is extended to its side on moderation, this is if the fracture is in the middle of the bone, but if it is near the origin of the femur then it is necessary to tighten a soft bandage either from wool or the like.
At the origin of the femur towards the groin so that the extension falls above and the other bandage below the fracture, and the same if the fracture is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the extension falls below, then the physician smooths out the bone with both hands until he returns it to the example of the natural shape.
The bone then unites well, and it is then necessary to apply bandages and traction. If a hot tumor does not appear in the member, if it does appear, leave it for days until the hot tumor subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for traction, it is necessary to wind a broad, rigid bandage twice or three times around the fracture, and what remains should be left. Then wind the leg until the heel reaches the origin of the thigh, and insert a long thread between the femur and tibia near the knee from below, making the ends of the thread protrude above on both sides.
Then wind the remaining bandage around the leg and thigh, then place splints in the same place on the femur as the fracture, making one splint on the tibia, and then stuff the gap between the femur and tibia with a soft linen cloth until the traction is equal.
Then begin the strong traction from the middle at the site of the fracture, three or four turns. And as you move the bandage away from the site of the fracture, let your traction be less and softer and looser.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread you inserted between the femur and tibia, tie them to the remaining splints above, then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the ankle, and pull them to also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not move from its place, then leave the traction on it as long as there is no sign of inflammation or tumor or swelling in the member, if anything happens from this, proceed to resolve it and repair what happened from it all as we mentioned repeatedly.
If there is a shard in the bone, it is necessary to remove it if you can, otherwise, cut through it and remove it, and treat the wound like what was mentioned before until it heals. This fracture may be tightened from the femur without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the arm and leg, if it is set with [the bone] of the thigh, it does not cause lameness for the patient, but if it is set alone without including the leg then its owner will be lame forever.
Know that the thigh takes fifty days to heal, or a little more or a little less, all of this according to the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
The Fifteenth Chapter.
In a fracture of the knee cap.
The knee cap rarely fractures, but it often suffers from contusions. If it does fracture, it is either a split or fractured into pieces, and this can occur with or without a wound.
And you act on all this by feeling, and set it by bringing together the separated parts with your fingers until they join and align according to what you are able to achieve with gentleness, care, and skill. Then apply a bandage and place a round splint on top of it if needed.
And tighten the bandage above the splint, and pay attention to all conditions that we described in other fractures such as hot swelling and similar things by responding to each complication with what is appropriate for it until it heals.
The Sixteenth Chapter.
In Setting a Leg Fracture.
The leg has two bones: one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula. Both are subject to the same types of fractures as the arm bones. Therefore, setting a leg fracture is like setting an arm fracture; the process is the same. If both bones fracture, the leg turns in all directions. If the thinner bone fractures, the leg bends forward. And if the thicker bone fractures and it's from the lower part, this is something that is not hidden from you.
So, use strapping and alignment and tying splints accordingly, except that if the leg fracture is an open one with many fragments, the strapping should be less and lighter, and you should be gentle in setting it.
In the leg, there is something additional to the arm; and when you have finished setting the splints and all your work, take two pieces of pinewood that are used for smoothing the partitions which are placed between the gaps in the planks or be made of palm branches or similar things.
And choose from them what has a slight thickness and is not thin, and let their length be along the length of the leg from the knee to below, then wrap each one with two bandages lengthwise, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
And let them be from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two splints together in three places from their ends and the middle because by this harness the leg is prevented from tilting right and left and it becomes well-secured, and one may use a gutter of wood along the length of the leg.
And it is placed in it to protect it from movement and most of all it is necessary to do that in a fracture if it is accompanied by a special wound, then inspect the leg every day and pay great attention to swelling or puffiness or anything like that. So when something of that happens, meet it with what is appropriate until it heals, and the bone of the leg heals in thirty days or so.
The Seventeenth Chapter.
In the fracture of the bones of the foot and fingers.
As for the heel, it is not subjected to fracture of the bone, but as for the bones of the foot, they can be fractured, and also the fingers, rarely are they fractured, but rather they are subjected to bruises in most cases.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs and you see that some of those bones have overlapped each other, then let the patient place his foot on the ground upright like when walking, then you put your foot on what has risen from those bones and step on it and level it.
Until it returns to its place, then carry the bandage and poultice from above and put under the sole of the foot a small board with two flat heads, then tighten the foot tightly after wrapping it with cloth and everything else you need.
If three or four days have passed, then release the bandage and you will find the bones level whether they were broken or dislocated, so work as you see fit. As for if some of the fingers are broken, force them and make them even according to what I described to you in setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along the length of the finger and let it be slightly wider than it, then place under the foot this board that I described to you and tighten it tightly. If two or three or more bones are broken.
Then on each finger make a splint the size of a row in a soft cloth and tighten the board at the bottom of the foot, so that each head of the two heads of the board is outside the sole of the foot so it adjusts well.
And it is necessary for you not to forget to observe all that we have mentioned to you in all fractures from the symptoms that we have mentioned and meet each symptom with what is appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of a woman's pubic bone and the man's.
When the woman's pubic bone is fractured, place her sitting and then bend her slightly towards her back, little by little, and hold her from behind, then make the midwife fill her vagina with cotton until it fills and becomes in her vagina like a ball.
Then shake the woman and raise her hips slightly, little by little, so that that cotton comes out until it is at the entrance of the vagina like a ball and the broken bone returns, then place a splint on her back, and when she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently until she urinates.
Replace it in the manner in which you first introduced it, then return to its original location, do this for seven days or thereabouts, for it will heal; and if you wish, take the bladder of a sheep, tighten its mouth with a short tube and introduce the entire bladder into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vagina, for the fracture returns, then stitch it with cotton and keep it as described for days until it heals; as for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, it is used in its healing.
And its treatment is as we described for the hip bone, and it is not hidden from you that what is right in these strange fractures which rarely occur, especially to someone who has some experience, and understands this book well, because the fracture itself indicates this in most cases.
On the right path in its healing and tightening, so understand; as for mentioning the man if his thigh bone is fractured, a  "saddle" (a type of splint) is inserted into it, then a layer of cloth is placed on top of it and bound, and left for three days or thereabouts until it heals.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
In the Setting of Fractures When Accompanied by Wounds
It is necessary for whoever presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large like the thigh bone or humerus or similar, that you act quickly and dissect it from its time if the conditions of the surgical incision assist you as we have presented before.
If the wound is bleeding, it is necessary that you act quickly to stop it by applying powdered saffron on it, if nothing else is available, and then take action in setting the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If a hot swelling does not occur, if a hot swelling occurs, leave its treatment until the ninth day until the hot swelling subsides and do not touch it on the third and fourth days at all, for it will be exposed to poor conditions.
If the broken bone is exposed through the skin, it is necessary to gently push it back into place and smooth it with your hands as much as possible. If you cannot push it back and smooth it with your hands, use this instrument which is made of iron, seven or eight finger lengths long. And lay its width across the size of the wound.
And for this reason, the physician should take three or four of them according to his need for treatment in each type of fracture, and let them be round with slight thickness so that they will not bend when pressed upon during work.
And their ends are sharp with a curve at their tip and their top is thicker and from its middle to the bottom they become much thinner. And this is its image:
And it is called in Greek "perim" meaning a small lever, so it should be that its sharp, curved tip is placed on the end of the broken bone and pushed with it once until when the bone returns and reaches some level of straightness, then smooth the edges of the fracture against each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument did not grasp it well, then it is necessary to cut that piece of bone until the instrument can grip it. If you cannot push the bone back into place as described above, then cut it into pieces like those we mentioned before.
Or spread it with one of the saws as much as you can, then remove what remains in the bone from roughness and thin bark. And if you push the bone back and find after its return, intense and painful throbbing, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you manage to push it back into its natural place, then do so because you will be doing great benefit to the patient. When you have finished setting the bone, dip a piece of cloth in black astringent syrup, especially if you are in summer, and do not put any poultices or anything greasy on the wound.
So that it may not become rotten or corrupted, then use splints while you are free from setting the bone and leave the wound open by cutting a hole in the dressings with a pair of scissors to match the size of the wound.
And be extremely careful not to tighten the wound with a fracture, for often ignorant physicians have done this to their patients, causing either death or gangrene or crippling. Let your tightening be gentle and loose, unlike the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the bad symptoms we have described, and he finds pain in the place that is disturbing, it is not necessary to put splints on it. Make for him dressings from stiff cloth in the place of the splints and tighten them.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to generate pus, remove from it the cloth you placed on it with the drink then use the poultice and ointments that we are accustomed to treat wounds with like the four-part ointment and the like, and it is necessary for you to loosen the bandage.
And inspect the wound every day at evening and morning until it heals, and it is necessary for you to set the limb so that it may drain pus downward easily. If the wound has passed many days and it has not closed up or the pus has not stopped, know that there are small bone fragments in it.
Then it is necessary to search the wound with a probe, whatever of those fragments that are white and clean, remove them and take them out, and whatever of them are not white and clean and are pressing on the limb and causing pain, then cut them and extract them as best you can.
If a cold, gangrene or another kind of corruption and putrefaction appears in the wound, then it is necessary to meet each case with its appropriate treatment which I have described in its chapter.
When a large bone, such as the femur or humerus and similar large limbs, is broken, do not attempt to pull it or remove it. For often that causes death, so leave it until it decays; perhaps it will fall out of itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On Treating Complication That Arises After Some Fractures.
Complications often arise after a fracture heals, especially near the joints, which disfigure the limb and may prevent it from performing its natural function.
If the complication is pus, use medicines that tighten it like frankincense, mastic, bitter root, and quinine by taking some or all of these and mixing them with a binding syrup, egg white, or vinegar.
And apply it to the complication with a bandage and tie it tightly, leaving the bandage on for many days then remove it and reapply another until the complication disappears, God willing, or fasten a sheet of well-sealed lead over it.
For lead has a property that removes everything that is in the body's parts, if the complication has hardened and worsened, and necessity compels its removal, then cut into it from above and remove the excess or scrape it with some tools until it disappears and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Treating a Fracture If It Breaks and the Limb Remains Weak After, Different From Its Original Nature.
If a fracture breaks and the limb remains weak after, it is due to many reasons. One of them is either due to an excessive tying of the ligaments and their fastening in a way that is not appropriate, or due to overtightening of the ligaments until food is prevented from reaching the limb, or due to excessive suturing, or due to excessive movements at an inappropriate time, or due to a deficiency of blood in the body of the patient and his weakness.
And the treatment for that is to nourish the patient and enrich his body until blood increases in him, and the use of the bath and introducing joy to him and happiness and the like, then bearing a sponge on the limb so that it attracts a lot of nourishment to it or to maintain its suturing with lukewarm water. Until food flows in it and it returns to its natural shape,.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In the treatment of a broken bone if it is bent and prevents its action as it should.
Then when a limb has been treated after healing, a crookedness or bump appears in the broken bone or it becomes complex and its image is disfigured because of that. Except that the limb did not prevent its natural action, then it is not necessary to accept the saying of one who claims to break the limb from its head and many ignorant physicians and practitioners used to do this in our country.
And this act is highly reprehensible and leads to great harm that is easily caused by the defect, but if the crookedness and complexity are gentle, then it should be sutured with water that has been cooked with soothing herbs like  comfrey, its root, crown imperial, and the like.
And it is bandaged with soothing bandages like  properly made diachylon or the mucilage of comfrey is taken and beaten with chicken fat and sheep tallow and bandaged with it, or ripe figs are taken and pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medicines called  "resolvents of induration.
And if the curvature is hardened by persistent gentle rubbing with the hand, and the movement of the limb is used in all directions at all times, then if the deformity has advanced and become severe, and necessity requires treatment with iron...
it is necessary to make an incision above it, and release the connection of the bone, and cut what remains of the curvature or bone with gentle incisions, and use gentleness and care in that, then treat the wound as mentioned before until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jawbone is a joint that moves out of its place, hindering movement and disfiguring the limb. It causes intense pain to the patient. When someone experiences a dislocated jaw, it is necessary to act immediately to put it back in place.
Do not delay it at all, for if delayed, swelling of the area occurs which makes returning the jaw difficult. Therefore, it should not be delayed or moved or stretched while swollen because it often causes spasms and painful aches to the patient.
But if this happens, it is necessary to quickly treat the patient, then leave him until the swelling subsides a little, then apply water and oil to the limb, then gently put it back in place, treating each part as mentioned in its place.
And I have arranged the chapters on the jawbone also according to what has been mentioned before regarding the fracture, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On Treating a Dislocated Lower Jawbone.
The jawbones rarely dislocate except in rare cases, and their dislocation occurs on one of two sides: either they move slightly from their places, relaxing a little, or completely detach entirely until they relax fully. Until the chest, so that the saliva of the sick person flows and he is unable to hold it back and cannot close his jaw, and his tongue trembles with speech. But if his vomiting was easy, then in most cases he may return to himself by the easiest means.
But if the dislocation is complete, it is necessary to hasten its return quickly and not delay it at all, which is that a servant grasps the head of the sick person and the physician inserts his index finger of one hand into the root of the jaw inside his mouth if the jaw is from one side.
Or he inserts both index fingers if the jaw is from both sides and the rest of his fingers from outside he adjusts with them, and he orders the sick person to relax his jaw and release it to go in all directions and the physician adjusts the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its return is difficult, especially if both jaws are involved, use a compress with hot water and oil until it is easier to return them and do not delay their return at all as we said. If they return and settle and the mouth of the sick person closes and does not relax, then place on them bandages of cloth with a lining made from wax and oil.
Then it is gently tied with a loose bandage, and the sick person's sleep should be on his back and his head elevated between two pillows so that he does not move his right or left side and does not try to chew anything but rather makes his food very soft.
Until the pain goes away and the jaw is firm, he should eat what seems good to him, and use it gently and not force his mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is healed and recovered.
If the return of the jaws is difficult if they were dislocated for a while and did not return to their places, it often happens that fevers and constant headaches occur and perhaps the belly of the sick person starts and he vomits repeatedly, so if you see this, know that it has been damaged.
Many often die from this within ten days.
The twenty-fifth chapter.
In the treatment of a fractured collarbone and shoulder joint.
As for the collarbone, it does not break from the inner side due to its connection with the chest, and it may break outwards, which is apparent to the touch. And when straightening it, the patient should lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the place with your hand, strongly pressing it down; then it will return to its place. Then put a bandage and support on it and tie it tightly. As for its end that follows the shoulder and connects with it, it rarely dislocates, and if it dislocates one day, it must be returned and straightened as mentioned above and what appears appropriate to you.
Then put a bandage and support on it and tie it tightly. Then command the patient to adhere to rest and stillness until he heals. And with this treatment specifically, the shoulder end is also returned if it moves from its place.
The twenty-sixth chapter.
In the treatment of a dislocated shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder joint dislocates in three ways: one way is that it dislocates towards the armpit downwards, the second way is that it dislocates towards the chest, and perhaps it dislocates above the shoulder, which is rare.
And it does not dislocate backwards towards the shoulder blade, nor forwards towards the nerve place. And it is most likely to dislocate and come out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh because it comes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
As for those with much flesh, it is the opposite, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty. And perhaps a blow or fall occurs to some people so that the shoulder joint swells and a hot suppuration appears in it, and they think that it has dislocated; therefore, it is necessary to examine it until you are certain of its health.
Then in his treatment, the jaw is advanced, and if it is below the armpit, if you bring together the dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, you will find a clear difference between them and you will find the head of the shoulder to be twisted there, and under the armpit upon touch, the head of the shoulder like an egg.
And the sick person is unable to raise his hand to his ear, nor to move it in all movements, and if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent to the touch, not hidden.
And this dislocation may be easy to return if it is soft or the patient is a boy, and its return is that a servant raises his hand above, then you put your thumbs under his armpit and raise the joint forcefully upwards to its place, and the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he brings it down and it returns quickly, if it does not return with what was mentioned and the dislocation has been for many days, then it is necessary for the patient to bathe in hot water and use the poultice that softens and relaxes like cooking the roots of  cinnamon, licorice, and crown imperial in water and use.
Then the patient lies on his back and a ball of wool that is moderate between soft and hard is placed under his armpit, then the doctor puts his heels on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his leg and it returns to its place, if you wish, return it in this way which is that you bring a man taller than the patient and stand him on one side of the ribcage and he puts his shoulder under the armpit of the patient and raises his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand downwards to his abdomen, if the patient is light, it is necessary to hang something else on him to weigh him down, then he returns the dislocation from his hour, and it may also be returned in another way.
And it is that you focus in the earth a long piece of wood whose head is round like the pestle of the mortar not thick nor thin, and then it is placed under the armpit of the patient after placing on the top of the wood a soft opening and the patient standing on the length of the wood.
Then you extend his hand down from the other side and he extends his body also from the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly. If it is difficult to return it with all that has been mentioned, then use this treatment which is to take a piece of wood about two arm lengths long. And its width is the measure of four fingers and its thickness is the measure of two fingers, it should have a rounded head to easily enter the depth of the armpit in this way:
Then a soft opening is tied on the rounded head so that it does not harm the patient, and then it is placed under the armpit and the whole hand and arm are extended onto the wood downward and the wood is bound to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a beam, and the hand is extended downward and the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side so that the joint enters in its hour. If it enters in any way possible then it is necessary to place under the armpit a ball of moderate size of wool.
Then the prepared bandage of flour dust with frankincense and egg white is applied to the whole shoulder as it rotates from above, then the ball under the armpit is tightened firmly and it is rotated by the bandage from above and his hand is hung up to his neck.
And he is left not to move his hand for seven days, and it is necessary that the patient's food be little until the limb strengthens as it is faster for its healing, then after the seven days or five it is loosened and practiced with movement. If it is fixed and has not relaxed, then he has healed.
If a joint dislocates repeatedly because of moisture exposure or another cause, then it is necessary to apply the kī with three safāfid as described in the chapter on kī. If this was done completely and the bandage was removed after seven days and the joint did not stabilize.
The bandage was reapplied and tightened multiple times, but it did not stabilize, fell, relaxed, and you could not raise it above. Know that the nerve in the head of the shoulder may have been severed, extended or relaxed.  Know at this point that the joint will never stabilize in its place.
As for the jaw that is near the chest and breast and behind its articulation, it is treated by pushing and stretching with the hands until it returns. All tightening and treatment are applied until it heals. If it appears after healing, stiffness in the limb and slower movement.
The patient should use the hot bath repeatedly until that stiffness softens and returns to its original nature.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Treatment of the Elbow Joint.
The elbow joint is difficult to dislocate, and it also returns with difficulty. It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is located under sight and touch so that any shape of dislocation can be seen.
And if the dislocated elbow is compared to a normal one, it becomes clear through visible signs of  the dislocation. The arm cannot be bent and his hand cannot touch his shoulder. It is necessary that you act quickly and return the dislocation from the moment it occurs before a hot swelling appears.
Because if a hot swelling appears, it is difficult to return the dislocation and may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backward, as it is the most difficult of all types of dislocations and the most painful. Often, death accompanies it.
If the dislocation is of a kind that can be reduced, let the servant extend both his hands and arms, and the physician place his hand above the elbow and below it, and he should push the joint with the thumbs of both hands or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
As for if the jaw is dislocated forward, it can be reduced by bending the hand once until it strikes with the base of the palm the shoulder which corresponds to it. If the jaw does not return, use strong forceful traction, truly, which is that two servants extend the arm.
And the patient be grasped by two servants also so that it does not depart during traction, and then the arm be turned in every direction after it is wrapped on his hand with a folded cloth or a wide piece of fabric. And when the physician begins with his hands to the joint, rub it with an ointment so that it would aid in the easy sliding of the joint.
Then he pushes the joint with a strong push until it returns, and after its return, it is necessary that a bandage be placed on it which contains pressure and drying with egg white, and it is tightened tightly and the arm is suspended from the neck of the patient and left for days.
Then it is released if the joint is fixed in its place, and the bandage is removed and left, but if you see that the joint has not become well-fixed, renew the bandage and the splint and leave it also for days until it becomes firm and then release it. If there was a swelling after the return of the joint and its release.
And if it is difficult in movement, let him use the soaking in the bath and gentle massage and fomentation until it softens, or put on the joint a sheep's fat udder and then bind it and leave it on for a day and night and then remove it and make him enter the bath. If he sweats, rub the joint with a moderate friction.
Then repeat the udder on him once and twice and thrice with entering the bath until it softens. If you wish to put on him wet bull fat mixed with butter and tighten it on him, do that for a few times because it softens and returns to its original state.
The Eighth and Twentieth Chapter
On Treating the Fracture of the Radius.
The wrist is frequently fractured and its setting is easier than that of other joints, except that it is necessary to set the fracture immediately after it occurs before a swelling sets in or a hot swelling appears.
In setting the fracture, you should place the injured person's wrist on a board and stretch their hand forward. The physician then places his hand on the joint prominence and pushes until it returns; however, it is necessary to look if the fracture has shifted into the palm of the hand.
If the fracture is protruding to the palm of the hand, then let the patient place his back of the hand on the board when stretching and setting it; if the fracture returns from its position then leave it as it is; otherwise, bind it with a bandage to relieve the swelling.
And leave him alone as he cannot bear it and you will not be able to set it after a few days have passed except that the joint remains at its place and does not harm the patient except if the hand is stretched out and he is unable to grasp anything.
Then you will know that the nerve is severed or compressed, there is no remedy for it except to bind it tightly and perhaps it will be helpful and perhaps it will not help at all; if the radius is set then apply the bandage we have described, and tighten it and leave it for five days.
Then remove it and exercise the hand; if its movement is difficult and something from the limb appears, then soften it with warm water and rub it several times until it softens.
The Ninth and Twentieth Chapter
On Treating Finger Fractures.
The fingers may be fractured in every direction; if a finger is fractured towards the back of the hand or the palm, then stretch the finger and push the fracture with your thumb until it returns; then bind the tip of the finger and hang it toward the direction it was fractured.
Leave it for two days, then release and extend it until it becomes straight on the day. On that day, if it is at night, tie it in the same way. It will remain tied during the day and be trained with movement, and be tied at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And so do the same if it breaks inward to the palm, tie it towards the same direction and do with it what you did first until it heals. And do the same when it breaks in other directions.
The Thirty Chapter.
In Treating a Fracture of the Vertebrae.
When a fracture occurs in one of the vertebrae of the back or neck, if the fracture is complete or many vertebrae have moved from their places, there is no treatment because death quickly comes to the patient.
And the sign of that is that the patient's feces come out without his will, and he cannot hold it, and often some of his limbs relax, either his legs or his arms, or one of them. And if one vertebra has moved from its place, then many times more move.
And it moves in four directions: the one that moves backward is called a hump. And its treatment is to look at it; if the hump occurred from childhood, there is no treatment for it, nor any cure at all. But the one that happened because of a fall or a blow and the like...
...The ancients mentioned it with types of treatment in lengthy discourse, most of which is not much use. I have shortened it from what is enough to replace much of what they brought.  I approached the meaning and explained it, and depicted the instrument differently than how they described and explained it.
I say that the hump that occurs in front of the chest, there is no cure for it, nor any healing from it. And so with the ones on the sides as well. But what is treated is the one that occurs in the back, especially what I describe, which is to extend the patient on his face.
On a bench, level and near a wall, spread out beneath it a damp rug so as not to hurt his chest; then place a vertical plank embedded in a hole in the earth at one end of the bench toward his head and another plank toward his feet at the other end of the bench.
And a servant holds the plank and let it not be firmly secured in the hole, and another servant holds the other one in that position; then wrap around the patient’s chest and under his armpit with a soft, tight bandage and extend the end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tie it there.
